The BONUSES declared on the Company’s Whole Life New Distribution Policies of only Five
Years’ standing range from £1 19s. Id. to £3 12s. 7d. per cent, per annumn, according to age.
A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,039 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash value of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments —more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE,
MEDLOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform almost
adjoining, and its level land proves it to bo eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large—from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tenuis Lawns,
9*c-, if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
. The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MELLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the P'irst Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
.years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-

Dr. Perry’s Pamphlet on Nervous Diseases:
Their a usb, Symptoms, and Treatment.
This pamphlet is specially written to show a method of
counteracting the ill effects of indiscretions in early life,
over-work, mental worry, &c., and contains a fund of use-
ful information for every sufferer from the following com-
plaints, viz : —Loss of Energy and Will Power, NERVOUS
DEBILITY, Pains in the Back, Defective Memory, Specks
before the Eyes, Excessive Nervousness, Premature
Decay, Bashfulness, Excessive Heart Palpitation, Des-
pondency, Impure Blood, Liver and Kidney Complaints,
Contagious Diseases, and various other symptoms experi-
enced by Nervous Sufferers.
This Pamphlet is sent Free on Application,
and all in any way suffering from the above would derive
benefit from the perusal of this work.
If the patient’s case is of long standing, and, therefore
in need of treatment, he should lose no time in writing
me a letter detailing in full his symptoms, on receipt of
which I will at once reply, explaining the nature of his
complaint, and if I can effect a cure, the time it will take,
and the cost of doing so. My long experience in the
treatment of Nervous Diseases has made me thoroughly
conversant with all their peculiarities. I am prepared,
therefore, to
Guarantee a Permanent and Complete Cure in every case I undertake.
f invite the fullest investigation into my qualifications. For some time I was House Surgeon to the Great
Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of investigating the causes of Nervous Diseases, and
become perfectly acquainted with the most approved scientific and successful ihethods of affecting their cure.

THAT TROUBLESOME INFANT CONTINUED.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 31, 1891.
The Cheapest Freetrader on Earth.
The Freetrade system is admittedly a system
of Free Competition. So f<ar, in reality,
there is no Freetrade and no absolutely Free
Competition in any civilised land, but it is
the boast of England and its servile imitator,
New South Wales, that they approach as
near to it as circumstances will admit. They
are both in an embryotic state, so far as
their fiscal arrangements are concerned, but
in the latter country, at all events, the
fanatics of political economy see, or profess to
see, a financial millennium ahead—a halcyon
period when the products of all countries
will be admitted free of duty to Australian
ports, and all the world will compete with
this nation on equal terms. Then the wine
industry must go and the vines be rooted up
unless the New South Wales vigneron will
be content to toil as cheaply as the labourers
of Spain, Portugal, Italy, and Brazil, for the
consumer always buys the cheapest article,
and cheapness of production depends almost
entirely upon cheapness of labour. Then
tobacco cultivation will be suspended, and
the hitherto protected dairying industry will
die and be buried, and all the other trades
at present supported by tariffs levied in an
alleged Freetrade land must either work on
a cheaper basis than their competitors—or
else close up. The one absolutely reliable
fact about the Freetrade system is that the
cheapest producer must come uppermost
and the rest must go to the wall. It is this
cheapness which is the stock argument of all
Freetraders. The farmer is always told of a
golden age when he will get cheap machinery
from Europe, ana thus ruin the local imple-
ment-maker ; and the implement-maker is
told of a glorious time approaching when he
will get an abundance of cheap foreign grain
from India, and thus wipe out the farmer.
The tailor hears that the local bootmaker is
his enemy, because his boots are dearer than
the imported article, and the bootmaker is
warned of the evils of the other man because
his products are more costly than the
shoddy of Manchester. To every separate
individual the ta e is told of how he can get
cheap, slave-made goods from abroad, and
thereby avoid the extortions of —and thus
practically abolish —some other local indivi-
dual, and as the other individual has to
make his profit in like manner by abolishing
him, Freetrade —as applied to Australia—is
practically the doctrine of universal aboli-
tion. For only the cheapest man can
survive under the regime of the Freetrade
prophet, and as the Australian is about the
most expensive worker on earth he must go,
no matter who stays.
» * #
The Australian is about the most expen-
sive labourer on earth —the Chinaman is
probably the most inexpensive. The two
men occuny the extieme ends of the
industrial scale. . , So far, the struggle.
between the Mongol and the Freetrader of
New South Wales has not even commenced,
for the former has been busily employed
wiping out competitors nearer home. The
cheap Englishman gained a hold upon his
country forty years ago, in the days when
the son of Confucius knew practically
nothing of commerce, manufactures, or
arts, and the Chinaman had to extinguish
this intruder in his own land before he could
compete to much advantage in mote distant
regions. At present the work is proceeding
gaily. The Buddhist has learned only the
rudiments of industrial science, but already,
by virtue of the great Freetrade axiom by
which the dearest is taken and the cheapest
is left, the Briton is going fast. The model
English mercantile man in Shanghai—the
one who knows three or four languages and
is competent to take complete charge of a
large trading business—demands some £6OO
per annum ; the Chinese merchant has an
equally capable and equally intelligent
manager who receives £3O a year, his rice,
and the privilege of a shake-down in the
warehouse. The Chinese clerk and store-
man receives food, clothes, and the use of a
hard bunk, but wages, in his case, are
altogether omitted. The wealthy Chinese
merchant—the boss of the establishment —
lives anyhow, after the fashion of a pig.
“ This process is going on all over China,”
writes an American trader who once did an
immense business in Shanghai. “White
men employed and taught the Chinese and
the Chinese drove them out. We could no
more compete with them than we could over-
come death and fate.” The British Free-
trader in the Mongolian ports is just be-
ginning to realise the meaning of his own
doctrine of Free Competition, and the result
is about the most surprising thing that ever
happened to him.
» • •
The real merits of the Asiatic whom —in
the alleged Freetrade millennium—thepeople
of New South Wales must undersell if they
wish to save their industries from utter ex-
tinction, are only beginning to be under-
stood. He is a strange, untiring individual,
who seldom rests, and hardly ever sleeps,
and to whom recreation is an unknown
quantity. In the Chinese laundries of New
York and San Francisco work is never sus-
pended. Night and day the Mongol pursues
his ceaseless toil of, “ washee-washee-wash.”
For twenty hours per diem the same tireless
heathen labours at the tub, and for the
remaining four he sleeps among the soiled
linen, so that he may save the rent of sleep-
ing accommodation and the wear and tear of
bedding. In the underground haunts of
Chicago he turns his leprous kinsmen into
cash, and there night and day the diseased
wretches make cheap cigars which are after-
wards labelled as Havannahs and Manillas,
and sold in all parts of the world—in the
fashionable clubs and hotels of Sydney among
the rest. There is much solid economy
about the men who will make their dying
relatives toil for sixteen hours or more per
day, in exchange for rice and the shelter of
a subterranean cavern, and the New South
Wales citizen must learn to chain up his
consumptive friends and his crippled rela-
tions in a cell, and turn them into similar
sources of revenue, if he hopes to compete on
a Freetrade basis. For there are no out-
ward evidences to show that there have been
dead bodies among the choice Havannahs,
and that a noisome corpse got mixed some-
how with the fragrant Manillas ; and anyhow
the question is one of cheapness, not of
corpses, and on a solid Freetrade system the
manufacturer who runs a staff of unpaid
lepers in a dungeon can easily keep ahead.
• • «
In fifty years, at the present rate of pro-
gress, China can manufacture for the whole
world. There is nothing extravagant in
this assertion. With modern machinery—
to say nothing of the machinery that will
exist in another half-century—a nation which
now numbers, say, 350,000,000, and which
is willing to work for 16 hours per diem, can
manufacture everything which can possibly
be wanted by the world’s population, and
much more besides. Therefore, whbrever
the Freetrade system prevails, the China-
man, as the cheapest worker on earth, can
wipe out and utterly extinguish every com-
petitor, and leave whole nations unem-
ployed. What he will be as a manufacturer
in the future can be guessed by what he is,
as an agriculturist, in the present. There is
no such patient* and laborious toiler on the
surface of the globe.
In China the owner of land who should not cultivate it
would he deprived of it. Fertilizers undreamed of in
Europe are used there, and the nostrils of a European or
an American are assailed with all sorts of odours at
every turn in city and country. All ordure is penuriously
hoarded and used. Every stalk of rice is planted as
seed, and replanted in water by human hands, and to
add one handful to a crop would not be thought un-
worthy of effort. Rain water is everywhere stored in
ponds or in water-holes for irrigation, anil in all casts
fish are grown in these reservoirs. Human hands do all
the work ; human backs bear most of the land burdens ;
human animals are the beasts that drag most of the loads,
where they cannot be transported on canal or river.
Horses, cows anil sheep are crowded out; they would
cost more than they would produce. Why should a
horse or an ass be called to bear a burden when there are
poor human beasts to bo. hail, in number sulticient to
build the pyramids or to drag mountains from their
bases, if the poorest of wages to recompense such a task
were forthcoming? There are practically no plant weeds
to be seen in the most thickly populated parts of China.
There is no room for them, anil they are completely extir-
pated in a land where agriculture is so minute that the
roots of plants are examined to expel or to kill any insect
or grub that would date to dispute a living with the
hungry lord of creation. Seeds are steeped in liquid
manure to force them to rapid and luxuriant growth.
Fertilizers are applied directly to the roots of plants, and
not placed on the surface of the land as with us, for in
the latter process is evaporation and waste. Man is (he
only weed tolerated in China, and he teems everywhere.
A population of S(K) to the square mile is not uncommon
in the best agricultural districts there.
When the biography of some individual,
who has built up a fortune by his own
unaided efforts, is written as a bright and
shining example for the coming generations,
the story of his rise is almost always the
same. He got ahead of his fellow-men at
the outset by abject economy—by living in
squalor and discomfort—by incessant toil,
and by abandoning almost everything which
makes life pleasant. But all these things
are comparative terms ; he was simply more
toilsome, more economical, and more squalid
than the rest of his kind, and if they had
unanimously discovered and resorted to a
still lower depth of meanness and economy,
then he must have i/escended to *a lower
level even than they—«or else have lived and
died a poor man, and a failure. The mil-
lionaire who made his first rise by wearing
old clothes would have been left behind in
the scramble for wealth by a race of compe-
titors as energetic as himself who wore no
clothes at all; the individual who earned
the first beginnings of his capital by
selling old horse-shoes picked up on the
highway and hoarding up the proceeds,
would have been regarded as an ex-
travagant spendthrift in a community
where his fellows all devoted themselves to
scraping up fertiliser from the gutters, and
turning it into cash; and he would have
gone the way of spendthrifts in general and
been a shocking example to all posterity.
The Chinaman is the model economist of the
earth, and the only reason why he has not
yet grown rich is that he is surrounded by
350,000,000 human beings as hideously
economical as himself. The successful
economist is a person who trades on the
extravagances of others, and in all China
there is no extravagant individual or class
to trade upon. But if the Mongol comes
fairly into competition with any white
nation, as he only can do under Freetrade
principles, then he will inevitably suck the
life-blood of his rivals. For as the most
grasping of Hebrew money-lenders is to the
most lavish member of the “juggins” order,
so is the educated Chinaman to the Hebrew
money-lender himself. It is a problem of
simple proportion, and the answer is easy.
* * *
The race that works most incessantly,
that does without most “necessaries,'’ and
that hangs on hardest to the proceeds, must
ultimately come uppermost on a Freetrade
basis, and the man from the shores of the
Yellow Sea fills the bill in all three in-
stances. He can dispense with light, air,
rest, and recreation, and almost with clothes
as well. He can sleep standing, or doubled
up in a cask, or crammed into a packing-case
with his naked feet propped against a nail.
He can travel in a fetid junk, piled up in a
heap, like dead meat. In California he is
ousting the white farmer and fruitgrower by
the simple process of paying from
two up to ten times as much for
the land as they can afford to offer, and
even then he realises larger profits than the
Aryan ever did. He is absorbing the fruit-
preserving industry by the same effective
methods, and the trail of the leper is over
the tinned peaches and pines and other
luxuries which the American mail-steamers
land so abundantly on these shores. He is
starting innumerable manufactures in his
own land, and already many of his products
are sold in New South Wales disguised under
English names. “As an industrial sup-
planter and trade absorber no nation of the
earth can equal his.” China has done more
in a quarter of a century to annex the
commerce and industries of the world
than any other land has accomplished
in a hundred years. And so long as
her products come in free it matters
nothing whether the Chinaman himself is
excluded or not ; the results are the same
whether the Mongol undersells his com-
petitors by goods manufactured on the spot,
or bv goods manufactured in his own Flowery
Land, and then exported. He can only be
dealt witli by two alternatives ; either he
must be shut out by protective tariffs, or he
must be competed with on even terms —
which means that his rival must become as
cheap, as squalid, as laborious, and as
miserably penurious as himself. The China-
man is an argument in favour of Protection
that cannot be overcome, even if all the
paper on earth is covered with Freetrade
logic, and all the shelves of all the libraries
in the globe groan under the burden of
tomes of Cobden’s philosophy.
“One Mighty Nation.”
Great is Diana of the Ephesians ! Greater is
the Allah of Anglo-Saxondorn, and the Times is
his prophet ! Listen, ye nations, for one speaks
as having authority, and not as the Jews ! ’Tis
the voice of the Cable that is heard in the land,
and he maunders dulcetly in this wise, and the
hearts of the Jingoes are glad, and the Anglo-
asses gather together at the street-corners of
Australian cities, and look exceeding wise and
are comforted: —“The Times, in a leading
article this morning, says that England and
Australia, no matter what reforms may result
from the piesent labour or other movements,
will always be part of the same mighty nation.”
Heigho ! and Alas ! but here tlni wish is father
to the thought, and there is no truth in either,
but much bunkum.
# # #
The mighty wheel of Time goes whirling
through Eternity in altogether different fashion
than, that affording prospect of “ everlasting-
ness ” to any nation or congeries of nations ;
and every dog, and every worm, and every
microbe, and every empire has its day—
and are not The great ocean of nothing-
ness is for ever dotted with transient
islands of apparent permanence for a brief
season, but the dissolving views of change
preserve not even a remembrance of their
shades. Nothing can die, says Lord Al-
mighty Tennyson, but lie answers his own
assertion in another poem in which he declares
that nothing can live ; and the musty annals of
history record only the mutations of empires,
and indicate but the possibility of remem-
brances of kingdoms age-long forgotten. The
tramp of the armies of Cyrus and Alexander
are mingled with those of Genghis Khan and
Hannibal and Caesar and Buonaparte, and
the last war between Turkey and Russia echoes
only the distant roar of eternal strife, and the
thunder of Plevna is only a little foam on the
crest of a time-wave in a mighty ocean which
stretches abroad into the farness of the un-
known. This Empire of England ! What is
it but a bulb-growth in a forest of mighty trees
whose forebears are now historical carbon
furnishing a little gas for present consumption.
Till the stars grow old, and the sun grows cold,
and the leaves of the judgment-book unfold, is
a point of time prophetic of the continuance of
only one empire—the empire of constant muta-
tion and unchangeable change.
* •
That which is born shall surely die ; and the
'Taw''holds"good both of empires and babies, and
ephemerae and apple-trees. In the great vast-
ness of eternity there is nothing good and noth-
ing evil ; for all good and all evil are merely
relative, and Nature only is. This little
England which so boastfully claims the God-
given dominion of the earth is herself but a sea-
dissevered bit of Europe. The Godwin Sands
mark a sunken earldom. The walls of the
Reculvers are eaten away, even within the
memory of man, by the salt brine of ocean, and
the fat earth of graveyards furnishes food of the
richest to the oysters that batten on the dead.
During the recent severe gales the sea ran high
along the coast, and Sandgate, a maritime town
in Kent, was well nigh inundated, the waves
encroaching upon the mainland, destroying the
alignment of streets and submerging the dwell-
ings. Canute’s empire was threatened in the
almost mythic days by this other conqueror
men call Neptune, and Victoria’s petty island
is a smaller realm by many a noble league.
But to the English mind, the infinitesimal
fraction of an infinite fraction of English soil
is vaster altogether than God’s great universe,
and the British half many million times greater
than the outlying eternal whole.
# # #
Men and nations endure just for so long as
they can endure ; just for so long as there is a
vital spark of endurance within them, and no
longer. All this hugger - mugging anent
grandeur and greatness and “everlasting”
empire syonymises a mighty fear, and heralds
to men the fact that the courage which is silent
because it is self-reliant has long fled, and a
base terror has taken its place in the national
heart. Editor Stead wrote recently in a ful-
some panegyric of Parkes, Rhodes, and Mac-
donald —(entitled, “ Three Empire Builders ”)
—that if Canada, Australia, and Africa set up
national house-keeping for themselves, then was
there an end for ever to the British Empire ; and
we answer yea, it is so. But, says Stead, and
the other fearsome Jingoes, this cannot be:
One speech ; one nation ; one destiny ! They
try to cheer themselves with the reiteration of
a possibility that looks not too probable. The
boy whistles with apparent bravery in the
dark—but it is only because he is shaking in
his shoes. Take up any recent English book
of biography, of fiction, of poetry —always
the same old song, always the same dreary,
wearying fetich— Nelson, the Goo-given hero
of England ; Wellington, a modern Alex-
ander, and what-not, and what-not, and what-
not. The challenge has become a scream, and
the courage of the true man is lost in the brag-
gart claims to courage of the shivering pol-
troon. Not thus Germany, not thus France,
not thus Russia—tor their day is not yet
come, nor the fruit ripe for plucking. But
England ?
# # #
It is a huge order, this “ eternal ” demand of
the Times for allegiance on our part. And
what good could we do England either, save to
serve as a cemetery for her effete sons when her
final hour has struck ? We are not, and cannot
be, England. As America is America, so are
we Australia, despite apparent similarity in a
few directions. There is no real identity
between us. We are rude, ignorant, barbarous,
uncultivated, unrefined, strong, self-resourceful,
standing erect in our own shoes. We know
nothing, and brutishly desire to know less. We
have no art, no literature, no architecture, no
traditions, no history, no social beauties or
amenities, no culture. England, on the contrary,
is polished, polite, learned, cultured, refined,
wealthy, weak, dubious, spineless and elegantly
decrepit. The English are versed in all know-
ledge—and all knavery. They know everything,
both of good and of evil, and life tb/them em-
braces every experience from organised philan-
thropy to the nameless abominations of Cleve-
land-street and Dublin castle, including the
charming peccadilloes of Tranby Croft. England
is the home of art and literature ; her glorious
land is filled with the achievements of Gothic
architect and slum-tenement builder ; her noble
history is stuffed with high-born outrage and
blue-blooded brutality ; her traditions glitter
with the sheen of swords in battle play ,-and are
sonorous with the clash of arms her social
manners and her social culture are of so high a
standard that men, when they consecrate their
lives to these elegant pursuits, have no time left
to be either human or honest; her women are
so beautiful and so delicately nurtured that only
American millionaires can afford to buy them ;
and she is, moreover, ruled by an aged lady
pre-eminent in grief, who has upwards of e'.evm
millions of money and whose offspring are so
numerous that hardly any high walk of life is
destitute of a right royal ornament and exemplar
for the encouragement and edification of a de-
voted following. Australia is so cursed and
England is so blessed, that any lasting (let
alone “everlasting”) union is impossible be-
tween them. Can you conceive Paradise chang-
ing locations with the Hall of Eblis ?
« « «
No! It cannot be —this eternal union
between England and Australia ! The former


country may be washed away by the sea, or
eaten piecemeal by Polish Jews and German
sweaters; she will disappear doubtless in a
blaze of many-tailed glory, consonant with her
high traditions, and her memories of Alfred
the Great, and Nelson, and other heroes, from
Henry V. to Albert Wettin ; but let her not
attempt to link her fate with that of a convict
colony whose grandfather wore a leg-chain and
whose highest intellectual effort to-day is
represented by the daily and w'eekly journalist,
of whom an English writer justly states his
nation’s opinion in these words : “ The pseudo-
intellectualism of the journalists (Australian)
with its harmful ignorance, or even more harm-
ful superficiality, is the wdll-o’-the-wisp offered
to the callow Australian intelligence as spiritual
companion and guide.” And further along the
same writer remarks that “culture” at
the Antipodes is in as bad a way as
“society,” and no good could possibly come
out of this Southern Nazareth. To all of
which we meekly bow our heads. We are
callow, and ignorant, and uncultured, very
aggressive and abominably arrogant, with
strength in our limbs and sap in our bodies, and
we yearn for neither part nor lot with the
superior person over the ocean, who has grown
so old and so weak-minded that she begins to
think that she is immortal. Youth and age are
bad yoke-fellows. One knows too much, and
the other may, perchance, ask too much. We
do not believe in the eternity of anything, least
of all in the permanence of the doddering grand-
motherly Empire upon which the sun is too tired
to set, and upon which he consequently never
rises. Until the stars grow old, and the sun
grows cold, and the leaves of the judgment
book unfold, and the fish of the ocean gnaw the
tombstones of British kings, and oysters wax
fat on the culture of the United Kingdom, and
the gentle crustacean meanders about the
traditions of England’s mighty past, and ghosts
and goblins gibber in the streets of Sydney ;
then, and not till then—if we live so long a
national life—will Australia worry about being
apart of the same “mighty nation” as the
little bit of land separated from Europe by the
“encroaching sea,” and coloured red on maps
of English manufacture.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Labour and Imperialism.
The proposition recently advanced by the
New South Wales Labour Party that a Labour
candidate should be run in opposition to Premier
Dibbs at the Murrumbidgee is another striking
evidence that the third section of the Assembly
badly wants a competent leader —or even an
incompetent one. For no leader, however
foolish, could well make so many mistakes as
can be accomplished by a caucus, and the
scheme of ousting the head of the new Ministry
is unspeakably bad in every way- —bad if it
fails, even worse if it succeeds. A few months
ago the object of the party was to obtain certain
democratic concessions from Parkes, and then
after many weeks of weary labour it gave way
to a sudden burst of irrational anger, and
cut down that ancient political plant just
as the crop was about ripe. It was a very
scanty crop and of exceedingly little use,
but still its exact value had been apparent
almost from the beginning, and with this
knowledge at command the party had toiled
manfully to cultivate Parkes, and water him,
and bring him to maturity. After Parkes’
fall the Labour delegates wanted to obtain the
same concessions from Dibbs, and if it suc-
ceeded in abolishing him—as it apparently
hoped to do—at the Murrumbidgee election
that chance would also be wiped out by its own
act—to say nothing of the chance that the
party would be wiped out also. The fact that
such an idea could be entertained showed a
woeful want of calculation and intelligence.
The first object of the democratic section of the
House should now be to get the principle of
One-man-one-vote passed into law at the earliest
possible opportunity, so that, should a dissolu-
tion come at some unexpected moment, it will
get a fair field in the scramble that will ensue.
It has already thrown away whatever chance it
had of obtaining this concession from the old
Parkes Cabinet; if it succeeds by any chance
in stranding the Dibbs Cabinet at the very
beginning of its career the dissolution may
arrive at any time. Then the battle will be
fought over again at the polls on the old pro-
perty basis ; the “ National Association,” with
its elaborate machinery, will raise a more solid
vote in the interests of monopoly than ever was
raised before ; and the Labour members will
appear before their dispirited supporters
as a body of politicians who have done
nothing and elaborately fooled away every
chance that presented itself. A new party
which goes to the country without a single
success to its credit stands a fair show of never
coming back; and it is absolutely essential that
the Labour representatives should effect at least
one amongst their long list of reforms by way of
giving a reason for their existence. The party
has drifted into a strange and anomalous situa-
tion. If, after having backed Parkes, the
bigoted Imperialist, so long and so unquestion-
ingly, it sacks Dibbs, the avowed Republican,
without a trial, it thereby appears before the
country as the tool of the monopolist and
K.C.M.G. section of the nation. If, after
professing to be indifferent alike to Free-
trade and Protection and supporting- Parkes’s
Foreign-trade policy—avowedly out of sheer
indifference, it fails to support in turn his
successor’s Protectionist policy—also out of
indifference, then it will have to go before the
country as the agent of Calico-Jimmyism,
and the enemy of local industry. The only
remedy for this state of things is to start
the party on an entirely new basis. Let every
member of it vote on the fiscal issue according
to his own convictions—it has already been
used too long to prop up the great York-street
importing monopoly, and has gained nothing
by it. Let it bring in its own One-man-one-
vote Bill as a private measure which will stand
on its own merits instead of hanging on to the
tail of M'Millan’s cheap imported slop coats ;
and then if the scheme js placed in a practic-
able form the party w’U at least have the
credit of it, and if it fails, the responsibility
will rest, not on the Labour members,
but on their opponents; and the country
will fully appreciate the fact. Hitherto
the third party has placed itself in such
a position that it had to choose between
supporting a foolish and almost abortive measure
or else voting against its own principles, which
was little better than choosing between two
forms of suicide. Further, let it introduce, on
its own sole responsibility, Bills providing for
the abolition of party government and for the
introduction of the Referendum—two schemes
which are of more importance than even the
One-man-onc-vote policy—and if it fails the
country will send it back some day with a
majority that will command success. And,
lastly, let it avoid gaining a ruinous notoriety
by needlessly standing forth as the enemy of
the first Republican Premier of Australia, and
thus becoming a cheap advertisement for Im-
perialism and grovel. G. R. Dibbs may or may
not be an ideal Republican—time alone can
show ; but any kind of a Republican is better
than none.

The Queen’s Health.
A correspondent writes to The Bulletin in a
jubilant strain, to point out that sundry Labour
members of the New South Wales Assembly
have publicly declined to rise up when the
Queen’s health was drunk at various banquets,
and have thus trodden heavily on the rich and
loyal section of the company, and made them
feel bad. Possibly this paper should feel jubi-
lant over the matter also, but it doesn’t. The
alleged Republican who goes to a banquet
where he knows this ancient ceremony will be
gone through, with a view to objecting to it,
presumably goes for the sake of personal adver-
tisement ; if he only wants to adhere to his
democratic principles he would do it better by
staying away (if he can’t get the promoters to
omit the toast) and leaving out the advertise-
ment. The Protestant who can’t express his
sentiments except by going to a Catholic
church and there publicly cursing the Pope, or
the alleged Christian who feels it incumbent on
him to obtrusively exhibit his New Testament
in a synagogue so that he may know he is a
Christian, is generally hollow. If a demo-
crat finds the weary doggerel of “God Save
the Queen ” thrust on him in theatres and
public places where it has no connection
whatever with the business on hand, he
has every right to protest; when he goes to
haunts where that same doggerel is the prin-
cipal and only real business of the evening, he
has no more right to raise objection than he has
to visit General Booth, and then jerk mud at
him, as a testimony against the Salvation drum.
The man who objects to another man’s fad
when it is thrust upon him is distinctly justi-
fied ; the one who goes in pursuit of the other
man’s fad for the express purpose of firing
bricks at the clay idol is not justified. Even
the Chinaman has a right to his own Joss—in
China. Moreover, the world is trodden hard
with alleged reformers who do nothing for their
cause except level petty insults at the other
side, and it is all time and energy wasted. The
republic is not one whit nearer though 100,000
republicans go to superfluous loyal feeds, and
refuse to drink Queen Victoria’s health.
And if 100,000 republicans refuse to take the
formal and absolutely meaningless “oath of
allegiance ” the result is generally just the
same—it is a big advertisement at the moment,
and the advertiser talks of it for the rest of his
life and does no other good whatever. The
men who will bring along the Australian
Republic will be too busy with the great work
to stop and argue about miserable trivialities
like the Queen’s “health;” they will sooner
drink the utterly insignificant thing than argue
about it. They won’t trouble about oaths and
similar fooleries until the time comes to act;
every man means to be loyal to the sovereign
till the great commonwealth starts, and he may
as well take the oath —till then. The difference
between the two kinds of patriot is just the differ-
ence between Luther and the small, excited
Protestant who lets off squibs on the sth of
November ; between Washington and the boy
who threw mud at King George’s statue. If
Luther had been busy letting off fireworks
he wouldn’t have had time for a Reformation,
and if Washington had devoted himself to
firing mud at the brass eye of royalty he would
have had no leisure to revolushe. And for
these reasons The Bulletin doesn’t care
whether any particular democrat has drunk
the Queen’s health or not. We all drink the
Queen’s health—with one eye on the approach-
ing Republic.

Ways That Are Dark.
The case of Edward Bell, lately lagged in
Melbourne, is a curious instance of the pig-
headedness of the police-force, and the lack of
horse-sense among the administrators of the
law in general. Some time ago one Larck was
awakened by a burglar, and from that incident
Bell’s troubles originated. Larck, according
to his own showing, straightway rushed upon
the midnight prowler, and a fearful conflict
ensued. The visitor was a huge and brawny
ruffian, and they clutched each other with blind
ferocity, and hung on to each other, and ground
the furniture beneath their feet as they dragged
each other frantically about, and wiped the roof
with each other, and otherwise distinguished
themselves. But finally the invader tore him-
self away, leaving one of his coat-tails behind
him, and vanished like the reserve fund of a
land-bank. These facts, and the coat-tail, and
an elaborate account of the size and strength and
awful ferocity of his adversary, were duly
supplied by the prosecutor, and on the strength
of them the police arrested Edward Bell, a
miserable, weedy, stunted little vagrant, a
trifle over five feet high, and partially imbecile
as well. But his pants and waistcoat were of
the same material as the coat-tail herein before-
mentioned, and this fact gave him away.
According to the newspaper reports, the police
simply asked the prosecutor to identify Bell,
and he did it unanimously. The prisoner was
about 12 inches shorter than the perpetrator
of the outrage as described in the original
statement; he had no strength worth speak-
ing of; he was just about as ferocious
as a dishcloth at a maximum estimate ;
he hadn’t got the coat with the missing
tail, and though his pants were of the same
material, that could hardly be called evidence,
for there was no proof that the burglar’s pants
and coat corresponded. Also, the prosecutor
was near-sighted and wore glasses, and the row
took place in the dark when it was almost im-
possible to swear to anybody. Nevertheless,
he swore to the very first man shown him by
the police, and though the man was absolutely
and utterly unlike the person he described, his
statement was cheerfully accepted. The police
swallowed it; the jury took it in; the judge
bolted it whole; and the meek, stunted, little
man of five feet and a few inches is now doing
four years’ hard for being the huge, bulky
ruffian who burst into the heroic Mr. Larck’s
residence and fought like a tiger all over the
premises. Truly the law is a bigger fool even
than it looks.

The Plight of Capital.
The Age rises on its ear once more to complain
that the proceedings of the democratic party of
New South Wales will “drive capital out of
the colony.” We have heard this wail very,
very often till the wail itself has grown old and
haggard with much repetition, but the one
thing that is never explained is how or by what
process capital leaves a “colony.” According to
a recent estimate, the private wealth of New
South Wales is about £410,000,000, and of that
only £9,000,000 is moveable cash. The cash
may quit the country if the capitalist can get
hold of it, though much of it is in the hands of
Hebrews, old maids, preachers, and others
whose capacity for hanging on is simply
unlimited; but, granting that the foreign
money-lender could go round and scoop every-
thing down to the penny in the baby’s money-
box, there would be only £9,000,000 gone and
£401,000,000 would be left. He ean’t tear up
the roads and breakwaters and cathedrals and
things with his claws, and take them along with
him ; he can’t sling the lakes over his back, and
trail the railways and tramways behind him ;
nor can he dig up the mortgaged land, and bag
it, and hump it in a ship to England. These
things are the alien money-lender’s capital; if
he wants to take them out of the country he is
welcome—we want to see him do it. As to the
money, he can’t get half of it—it is too much
scattered ; and if he got the whole of it, it is
only about 2i per cent, of what we owe him,
and when he shifted it there would be a com-
mercial crisis, in which the other per cent,
would be lost. But it couldn’t be done, any-
how—it is a physical impossibility. Only the
other day we came across an alarming article
in a Maoriland paper about the awful manner
in which capital was quitting that country, and
we thought we had found the missing phenome-
non at last—but we hadn’t. It was a gruesome
account of how an imported British speculator
who owned a block of business property at
Auckland, being scared at the property-tax
proposals of the Ballance Cabinet, had sold
out and fled. We imagined for one dreadful
moment that he had taken the buildings along
with him—but it seems he didn’t. The capital
was too heavy to lift, and it is still standing at
the old corner to this day. It was only the
original owner who was gone, after all.
Slab from Bruce Smith’s speech on the Coal-
mines Bill;
Mr. Bruce Smith regretted that from the tone of the
majority of remarks made, it should appear that there
was only one class of men who were concerned in the bill
at all, and he would like to remind the committee that
there were others interested in coalmining besides the
men engaged below ground. There were.shareholders in
the companies working the mines, people who were not
always of the capitalistic class, but were often widows
and orphans, and it was their interests as well as the in-
terests of the miners which should be considered.
The Bulletin hereby informs Mr. Bruce
Smith that this widow and orphan racket
is too aged. We have looked through a good
many lists of shareholders in coal-mining com-
panies, and the widows are very scarce, and the
orphans are mostly old, wily, full-grown
orphans with a desperately keen eye for the
main chance. The genuine bona fide orphan
isn’t in it, probably because his trustee
took the shares, or else because he got
frozen out in some stock-exchange swindle
worked by somebody who got private informa-
tion from those “in the know.” As for the
widow, if her shares are worth anything at all
she got rushed into a second marriage almost
before her late husband was quite dead, and she
isn’t a widow any longer. Moreover, The
Bulletin never noticed Bruce Smith, in his
capacity as a director, taking any special in-
terest in widows and orphans : they arc only
trotted out when some Tory scheme wants to
be propped up. Therefore, it is our firm con-
viction that Bruce Smith himself is the widow
alluded to in the present case, that being the
only kind of widow that he ever seemed to take
much trouble about; and the or plum is also
Bruce Smith, there being no reason to imagine
that he is harassed over the troubles of any
other brand or variety of orphan whatsoever.
So often has the unsupported assertion of a girl
dragged a man to ruin that the ordinary mind
places little reliance on female evidence. The
case of Edwin Hickey, now doing eight years
in Armidale (N.S.W.) gaol, is one in point.
Hickey was a State-school master of apparently
good character. His accuser was a girl pupil,
whom, he alleged, he often had to admonish for
impropriety and forwardness. The trial fur-
nished ample evidence that what the girl didn’t
know in the miry byways of knowledge was
hardly ivorth mentioning. She was a precocious
person with* an ancient optic and an amazing
power of expression. Her story was that she
owed all Her evil knowledge to Hickey. The
defence was that she had trumped up the
charges as a matter of revenge. Hickey’s wife
stood by her husband to the last, a tolerably
certain sign that he was either a decent fellow
or a consummate hypocrite. There was a great
want of corroborative evidence on either
side ; the girl declared that she had been
seduced by Hickey, and Hickey denied the
accusation ; and that was virtually the whole
case. A verdict of guilty was returned, and
the prisoner was sentenced to eight years penrl
servitude. If Hickey had been in the witness-
box and the girl in the dock, the chances are
that she would have got the eight years instead
for perjury, for the bench which gave Hickey
eight years on a dubious-looking girl’s unsup-
ported testimony would as cheerfully have given
her the same sentence on his testimony. The
only visible difference between them was that
she got her case in first, and the average cow-
headed jury always believes the first story,
provided the accused isn’t a person of high
social position, and then it never believes any-
thing. Some day, perhaps, it may dawn on
somebody in power that no person should be
sent to gaol on the mere unsupported assertion
of any other person, but, so far, this idea seems
to have occurred to nobody.
Now that fresh confirmation has been received
from the Privy Council over the McLeod
bigamy case (though none was needed) to the
contention that the New South Wales Supreme
Court had no jurisdiction when bigamy was
committed in a foreign country, The Bulletin
asks Sir Alfred Stephen where was his
authority for hanging Black Peter, the
aboriginal, who inflicted wounds upon his lubra
on the Sydney side of the Murray, though she
did not die till she reached the Victorian side ?
According to strict law the crime was one of
unlawful wounding on this side of the Murray,
or of murder on the other. A murder is not
consummated until the death of the victim, and
the victim did not die within the jurisdiction of
the New South Wales Supreme Court. Yet
Black Peter was tried for murder and hanged.
And, by-and-by, The Bulletin will ask Sir
Alfred one or two other questions of an
analogous nature ; and will also inquire of the
Hon. the N.S.W. Attorney-General when he
proposes, by a short enactment, to repeal the
pertinent sections of Sir Alfred Stephen’s
Criminal Law Amendment Act. Purthermore,
it will seek to obtain a return of all decisions,
since the passing of that Act, which directly or
by implication have been pronounced, by the
Privy Council judgment in McLeod’s matter,
to have been illegal.
The following extracts from recent leaders in
the Sydney Daily Telegraph are interesting
reading :
The only way in which it is possible to form a stronsi or
permanent Government in the present House is by a
coalition on the basis of the fiscal Hiatus qtio. The policy
which the defeated Government went to the country
upon was endorsed by a substantial majority. ... In
the present House he (Mr. Dibbs) can only derive any
such power from shameless political conspiracy and by
occupying a position in regard to the labor party winch
will bring the Government of the country into contempt.
... . If he attempts to introduce a straight-out
protectionist policy, he will do so with the knowledge
that he has to face a House which, on repeatedly avowed
principle, is distinctly hostile.
“The policy which the defeated Government
went to the country upon was endorsed by a
substantial majority.” Yet, in the Daily
Telegraph of 4th July, there is a regretful
acknowledgment that the House consisted of 74
Protectionists, 64 Foreign-traders, and three
doubtful or unspecified politicians, leaving
Parkes and his policy in a minority of at least
seven. Therefore, by the unreliable and
wobblesome print’s own showing, Parkes’
policy was not endorsed at all, but unequivocally
condemned; and it was Parkes who hung on
by “shameless political conspiracy;” and it
was that same hairy philosopher who occupied
a position that was calculated to bring him and
his Government into contempt. It did bring him
there, too, in a most successful manner. In
fact he is there now.
Now t that the vessels of the Australian Tribute
fleet have survived the dangers of their trial
trips and of the vasty deep beyond the limits of
the measured mile, and their captains have got
over the volcanic disturbances in what the
sainted Sairey termed “the innards,” and the
luffs and mids are turning their optics in the
direction of the female offspring of the Tallow-
fats and the Woolbales, and the stokers have
ceased to pray for a speedy removal to Gehenna
in order to escape the infernallcr stokeholes, it
is about time these blawsted kawlinies were
favoured with some “ naval manoeuvres.” We
want something for our money, and we want to
see the torpedo-boats let go and miss every
aim, and the torpedo-catchers make a magnifi-
cent attempt at collaring the torpedo-boats and
getting a firm grip of nothing, and then finding
that Bradley’s Head has delicately insinuated
its rocky edges into the powder-magazine, and
the stewards’ pantry, and the lockers where the
first lieutenant keeps his mother’s Bible and his
best Darling Point girl’s photo, and outpour-
ings. And we want to hear the roar of our 29-
ton guns, and to compare their deep diapason
with the sound of heaven’s artillery, and Dan
O’Connor’s voice when he practises in his back-
yard the opening oration of his forthcoming
series of lectures on “Me and Other Irish
Pathriots,” and to know at what particular
round the average big gun of that calibre ex-
plodes into multitudinous atoms, so that we
may plant Parkes and Booth and the Echo
editor, and the errant William-goat, and other
perennial nuisances, in the neighbourhood of the
next that’s a-goin’ on the bust. We are hungering
to be informed on these points, and also at
what degree the tangent-sight travels to the
deuce, and whether it takes 45 hours or only
45 seconds for the concussion of a monster gun
to shatter the platform and to cause the
armoured plates to dissolve as rapidly as
Parkes’ assets, and whether it is three weeks
or a month before a cruiser roasts every
available stoker till he crackles and is done
brown. And we do not object to being in-
formed, furthermore, of the commanders’
capacity to make Port Jackson when South
Head Light is doused, as it would be in war,
and whether they would not one and all try
and come overland by way of the Gap ; and, in
short, we w r ant to know when Ad. Scott pro-
poses toblaze away a few thousand pounds’worth
of powder and blow up a few guns and a dozen
or two of seamen, to make a Sydney holiday,
and irretrievably to shake the hulls of the fleet
into the fathomless abysses of the rolling wave,
and to generally demonstrate his capacity to
fill at short notice the wards of all local
hospitals with shattered ruins, and every avail-
able dead-house with unassorted fragments of
corpse.

Premier Dibbs’ Manifesto.
Premier Dibbs, of New South Wales, has pub-
lished his political programme, and if he lives
up to that document, his Ministry will be about
the only democratic Government which has yet
existed iu Australia. The measures which he
hopes to carry in the fag-end of the present
wasted session are protection to local industry,
the abolition of plural voting, and the establish-
ment of courts of conciliation, and if these three
items of policy are pushed through, the results
achieved w ill be of more use to the country
than all the schemes propounded by Parkes since
the day when there was a Parkes for the first
time iu history. So far, too, the Protectionist sec-
tion has given some proof of its sincerity. It was
that party which first introduced the One-mau-
one-vote Bill before even the Labour-party came
into existence, and it was only by the most
vigorous exertions on the part of Parkes that
the measure was strangled and crowded out.
Then in after days Parkes borrowed this
scheme, and spoiled it in borrowing, and there-
after the House gave him credit for it, and the
Foreign-trade press blandly assumed that the
Protectionist-party was the natural and here-
ditary enemy of the project which it was the
first to advocate iu the House. And now,
strangely enough, this item of the old Protec-
tionist policy is shaken as a weapon at the
head of the party, and it is ordered to accept
its own scheme or stand the consequences.
Beyond this the Government proposes next
session to tackle the vexed question of law
reform, to push through the Local Government
Bill which Parkes has promised in vain for a
quarter of a century, to amend the laws
relating to masters and servants, and the
ancient-tyrannical statutes enacted for the sup-
pression of trades-unionism ; also to bring in a
Goal-mines Bill, and to do something of a
vigorous character for the regulation of the
bogus bank which takes iu the workman’s
savings on fixed deposit, and keeps them for
ever afterwards. Further, it undertakes to dig
up and amend the scheme of Australian
Federation, which Parkes so mysteriously
dropped, and then to submit it to the deci-
sion of the whole community, on a strictly
One-mau-one-vote basis. Consequently the
Dibbs Government is pledged up to the hilt to
democratic reform, and so long as it acts up to
its published programme it deserves the earnest
support of every democrat in New South Wales.
Unluckily, few Governments do live up to their
programme ; but still it is impossible to dis-
charge without a trial a Cabinet which under-
takes a great liberal policy, on the assumption
that it may not live up to its policy, else the
mere fact of a Cabinet promising reform would
be a sufficient reason for abolishing it, and the
only Cabinet which could be allowed to endure
Mould he the one which refused to promise
any reform at all. If the Dibbs Ministry
goes back on its programme in the
days to come then the same grave which
yawned for Parkes is ready for it. At present,
however, the one objection which is raised is
that Protection comes first on the list, and the
other items of reform afterwards, and the
Foreign-trade organs are already whooping up
the Labour-party on this pretext, iu the hope
that that section of the House M ill declare open
Mar, and thus do the dirty work of the great
importing monopoly. As it happens, however,
the country demands Protection quite as
urgently as it does electoral reform, and, more-
over—after a long and disastrous spell of
Foreign-trade—there is a £3,000,000 shortage
close at hand, and if Protection doesn’t come
M-ith exceeding great rapidity New South Wales
goes broke.
In the remote, outlying parts of Australia, the
cheap nigger and the cheap Chinaman are in
their glory. At Palmerston, the Chinaman
owns creation; at Thursday Island the
imported nigger runs the contract, but the
results to the white Australian are the same
either way. On the Island a white man is
getting to be a curiosity. In the principal
stores there are, on a steady average, some ten
low-priced Asiatics to each Australian em-
ployed. The ginger-beer factory is run by
Cingalese. The “cullnd pusson ” humps the
bales and hags on the wharf, and mans
the coal hulks. The drayman employs
a cheap black individual to drive his
cart. The fishing industry is run chiefly by
the same unobtrusive nigger. As for the white
population, it chiefly loafs about looking for a
job, and never finds it. Judging by present
appearances it never will find it. But as at
least one of the capitalists who employ nigger
labour in the seclusion of Thursday Island is a
Queensland M.L.A., and whoops more or less
in the House for the cause of national progress,
The Bulletin hereby invites the local Labour-
party to look him up, and make him reform, or
else cast him out ignomimously on his moral
and political ear.

Item from David Christie Murray :—■
Australia was built up by British enterprise and British
money. It is a British possession still, and without
British protection, British gold, and the trade which
exists between it and Britain, would be in a bad way.
The Bulletin hereby invites Murray to look
at the other side of the problem. Australia was
built up by British enterprise and British
money —by the enterprise and money of those
Britons who settled in Australia, and who,
with their sons, became the Australian
nation, and who now object to the in-
sufferable gall of the other Briton who
lid nothing for the country, but who now
passes himself off as the founder of it. As for
the gold alluded to, Australia has paid for it
all at a high rate, and if Britain isn’t grateful
for the interest, Australia declines to be grate-
ful for the principal. Up to now, Britain isn’t
grateful, so far as we have noticed—in fact, it
is the most ungrateful country we know of.
Also the trade comes under the same heading;
it is a mutual affair, and if Britain isn’t thank-
ful for its end of the trade, then Australia
declines to be thankful for the other end.
Some day we intend to write a pamphlet on
Britain’s awful want of thankfulness for the
profits of the Australian trade and the interest
on the money invested in this country, hut
meanwhile we respectfully draw Mr. I). C.
Murray’s attention to a side of the subject
which he seems to have entirely overlooked.

My Dear Moorabinda,—
N.S.W. Horticultural Society blossomed like a rose on
22nd inst.—the festive date on which it held its big
Spring show in the Centennial Hall—rather too gloomy a
place, let me object, to display the flowers to the best
advantage, for under the galleries especially - you had
really to peer to see what it was all about. There were
lots of specimens unrelieved by orchard produce, save and
except a branch of green oranges which gave one a colicky
sensation only to look at them—and the roses were utterly
utter. But what ugly names they give the pretty things 1
A really beautiful, deep-red rose was called the “R. A.
Williams,” and another, equally scenty and dainty, was
dubbed the “Dr. Hogg.” If ever I discover a rose I
intend to call it the “ Thomas Smith.” The decorated
tables—largely placarded “amateur division” in a quite
unnecessary - fashion—left more room for elbows and
plates, and were sometimes pretty - . One of them, with
small flowers, pansies, primroses, and so on, had aetinotus
mixed in, to the indignation of an old lady, who said she
couldn’t “ a-bcar to see wild flowers mixed up with
tame.”
As the Rose Show was the only event of importance in
a dull and dreary week, quite a gang turned up on two-
and-sixpenny- day. Lady Jersey, who set it all a-moving,
backed up by a blonde-haired aide (whom a wicked wag
has christened the “lemonade,” on account of his color-
ing), wore a figured pink silk, not precisely in the fashion,
trimmed with bands of black lace insertion and a narrow
black lace sash ; bonnet in black and gold, black fall,
cream parasol, and presented bouquet of lovely roses of
every colour. Mrs. A. J. Riley was in ccpper silk and
black lace and bonnet of white ostrich tips. Mrs. Calvert
(just from “ home ”) loomed up in black, and Miss Ethel
Calvert rose on the horizon in lavender-spotted white and
a pretty Paris hat. Their fellow-voyageuse, Miss Dick,
was in electric-blue trimmed with guipure lace. Mercury
hat in cream ; Mrs. Burnell, in cream and heliotrope*,
bonnet en suite ; Mrs. Broomfield in pink floral, black
crinoline hat trimmed with pink carnations; Miss M.
Windeyer, in sage-green, checked green straw hat
trimmed with light-green feathers ; the prettiest frock
there was a yellow mousseleine-de-soie embroidered on
the yoke and in a border round the hem with silk sun-
flowers and brown leaves, leaf-trimmed hat with knots of
brown velvet and a wreath of saffron roses. Marie Fraser
was there, chaperoned by one of the family of “ profes-
sional ” hunters, and wore a cream lustre cloak with a
light design of violets and leaves, with yoke of high collar
lined with pink satin, hat covered with white feathers. A
white corduroy gown, lined about with gold, rather sug-
gested breakfast china.
Heat and dust are almost come to stay now, so some-
body ought to let hot and gritty shoppers know that the
Country Milk Co.’s lounge, “The Cascade,” Elizabeth-
street, near King-street, serves very dainty, little after-
noon teas in its cool ladies’ room, with necessary
“toilette” attached, all shrouded from the main shop
with art curtains. Also, the “Cascade” strawberries and
Devonshire clotted cream are just exquisite, and a
“ hygienic ” item, not obtainable elsewhere, is any sort
of stewed fruit and rice—a favourite summer luncheon
order, as the manageress, Miss Saunders, informed
me. The shop is cool, large, and very ship-shape,
with a novelty in the form of a receptacle for parcels,
which can be left till called for—a most convenient thing
for all town travellers, who can send their packages there,
and so dodge the physical wear-and-tear of staggering
round the block weighed down with shopping results.
Mr. Harvey is the presiding genius of this convenient
resort.
A dinner-party to a rather heterogeneous mob, and a
daylight frisk to the members of the ’Varsity Women’s
Society and their hangers-on, comprised Government
House festivities for the last week, after which the Jerseys
had to fly to Moss Vale for a few days to recuperate. At
the Sydney ’Varsity entertainment were Lady Charles
Scott and her sister, Lady Barley, the three graces from
Bishopcourt, Mrs. C. B. Fairfax, &c. Lady Manning
(presidentess of the society) was not en evidence on
account of an attack of influenza. This complaint either
seeks to expend itself on society—at present largely
steeped in gruel and hot flannels and sneezing under the
Louis Quatorze bed-hangings—or else one doesn’t hear so
much about the other people, who have it and are out of
society. By the way, it’s amusing to see how some trades-
folk push their wares on the strength of “ La Gripe,” as a
well-known lady pronounces it. So-and-So’s biscuits are
a sure “ remedy;” a toy-seller says that his toys are the
salvation of convalescent kids; and, lastly, a veterinary
surgeon rises in print to point out that his latest horse-
lotion is a certain preventive, if you rub it in hard
enough. In spite of which you can’t enter into any public
place without being surrounded by most doleful sounding
“ a—a—tish—ows.”

goes on its anomalous way rejoicing!

set up for Soul hern vice-regality, a frisky married lady
and her gentleman “friend,” \ieing as to who should
show Lady Blank most attention. The query is—Would

“ The Dancing Girl,” at Her Majesty’s (which— the
theatre, not the girl—smells overpowerjngly of paint and
turps—it’s just had its spring coat of kalsomine laid on),
is one of those plays which try to insinuate that man—at
heart—is always a highly moral character yearning to do
good and only wanting a ghostly female in white to occur
at intervals to show him the way-even though he may
seem to be a wicked dook who can’t see the seamy side of
his vices until he has no more money to carry on with.
But I like the D.G. even though it was Miss Villiers—a
mature lady who talked like Harry Hoyte's “ Slender”
and walked like a Marionette—who put her up. Gwen-
dolyn Floyd made a pretty pathetic Sybil Crake and wore
a soft wh’ti clinging frock all through, but though there
wasn’t scope for much toggery in “The Dancing Girl,”
the company seems to let all the opportunity there is go.
Miss A’illiers dressed the 2nd and 3rd acts in rags literally,
even to a pair of soiled Suede gloves which she could
better have left off —and, as for the guests at Guise’s wed-
ding levee Before they'turned up the Duke soliloquised
something about “ My - friends will come dressed in expen-
sive clothing,” &c., and when the draggle-tailed gang
turned up, no self-respecting pawnbroker would have
given a florin for the whole bilin’lot of ’em.
On dit that the feature of a certain Australian city just
now is to see a professional man—a walking miracle of
how to cut a dash 011 nothing a year—tearing round in a
spiff turn-out, a pair of nags, with a cocky-looking Tiger
behind, and a charming girl on the front seat, whom he
is endeavouring to fascinate per means of the trap, the
trotters, and the Tiger. But the cream of it all is that
the entire high-flying panorama is put up by aspeculative
Israelite, who won’t present his little bill until the gilded
prize is hooked 1 What a happy bride she ought to be—
rushed into matrimony on a time-payment turn-out, laid
on by Moses Solomon Cohen !
What I want to enter into is the feelings of the Gaiety
lady in tights when she gets a note calling herself a lovely
creature (with all her paint and gold wiggery on), and
begging her to accord her fond worshipper a daylight
interview. If she does so, painted and wigged as ho
adored her, what a ghastly - spectacle the cream and
carmine will look in the sunlight! And if she takes off
the wig and the paint and comes out in her own mouse-
coloured hair and paint-worn visage, why, she must feel
what a how ling disappointment on two feet she is to her
moon-struck Johnnie, and no woman in the wide world
likes to feel like that. When you seriously come to think
of it, it must be rather awful to have people hurling
bouquets and adoration at you as a radiant blonde with
padded extremities, when behind the horsehair and the
pink dye you know that you are a black-haired, yellow-
faced croppy with limbs like knotted string. The sensa-
tion must be something like lending your soul to some-
body else, I should think.
Talking of things V.R., one Australian Governor is—er
—a little near, and so are his odd-sized suite to the last
man. At a bazaar held in Barataria recently his Excel-
lency - ordered “ sixpence-worth of sugar-plums,” which is
the vice-regal English way of asking for “ ’arf-a-pound of
toothache’’—tendering a shilling in payment, which mass
of capital the horse-leech’s daughter in charge slipped
into tier pocket on the bazaar principle of “no change
given.” But his Ex. waited humbly for that sixpence
until the mob began to whisper loudly “What’s he wait-
ing for?” upon which He (large-capital) mournfully moved
on. Beautiful example of thrift! It is at times like these
one reverently acknow ledges how - well the Queen is some-
times represented!
I am glad to hear that at a recent ball held at Adelaide
Government House, Lady Kintore requested that none of
her guests should wear black gowns—l am only surprised
any of ’em wanted to. The black ball-frock is a blot on
our social life that ought to be put down with a strong
hand, and hit hard when it is down, and why any woman
(I except, of course, elderly - parties) who can afford
sprightlier tints ever gets herself up like Hamlet’s aunt I
am at a loss to understand. Black is supposed to whiten
the skin, but it aiso accentuates every hollow in face or
neck and piles age onto the wearer like Father Time run
mad. Also, if your maid doesn’t sponge the bodice
now - and then, people are constantly reminded that
you’ve worn the dress before- a suspicion that the strong-
smelling black dye further confirms. And, further, to
catalogue its drawbacks the black evening dress being a
great “stay - er” is always the dismal covering of the
woman who hacks after society even though she can’t
afford it—who always plumes herself as she looks in the
glass that after all she is dressed deoollette and in lace
like the rest of the world, hut who seems to have written
all over her that she replaces the Y-shaped bodice for long
sleeves, and promenades in her decent black skirt during
the day-time ! Now I wonder if these are a few of the
reasons why Her Ladyship of South Australia objects to
receive black gowns?
I hear a harrowing tale of two hivin’ ’arts which beat as
one, but which unwisely put in a couple of days at a
relative’s house, with the result that the rosy fetters are
broken and love a blighted quantity. Really and truly,
the best possible plan for a fianeb to follow - if she wants to
be sure that she and Oussie are the right connubial
height, is to put up for a week in the most monotonous
house available, and calmly await symptoms. I knew a
young person who stayed ten days in the same house as
her fianed, and she came back (as I expected) a disengaged
wreck. She said ; “ There he was from sunrise to sunset
ogling me at breakfast, dinner and tea. I daren’t walk an
inch with anybody but him, and when jaunts were arranged
he was monotonously apportioned to me ! Tt was right of
the hostess, of course, but I felt so sheepish that I believe
nated him in 48 hours. It w - as a sort of dress-rehearsal
for the honeymoon, I felt, only one another all the time !
If brides feel as desperately anxious to get away from
proprietorial airs and graces on the part of a man who
has two plates of porridge for breakfast and a pound of
chops, and whom one hears scolding the housemaid about
his shaving-w ator, I don’t want to he one, that’s all
‘•And did ho ever chance to see you with your hair out
of curl?” said I, just to give the other side a show—but
echo had to answer me that question, of course.
But it's queer how monotonously engaged couples
disengage when one of the parties go away— especially on
a long sea trip. Absence makes the ’art grow fonder (on
paper), hut in real life this pretty fiction has to right
about face, judging by results. So fatal is sea-air to vows
of undying constancy that I know one or two men who
have accompanied their inamoratas “home” in prefer-
ence to risking a brief epistolatory snub from the other
side of tiie world, assuring “Dear Mr}' Jones” that her
feelings have undergone a change. Now, why is this
thus? Are the gold buttons on the steamer’s officers
wholly responsible? or is it that, with the average
woman, its “out of hug, out of mind,” if one may so
pervert it ?
A ring of tip top Duehesses lias formed in London to
keep out of proper society some of the weirdilics who get
“asked’ by Wales, an invitation from whom doesn’t by
any means confer the catchet of good form, dontoher-
know ; and, in a tin-pot way, we have the same sort of
thing in Australia. People who are cordially bidden to
the various Government Houses are not invited by a ring
of exclusive families—the sort of folks who grow high
trees round their wall and don't dine with their windows
open onto the street whom you can count on your
fingers comfortably. “Are you going to Government
House?” is socially equivalent to asking “Were 3on at
the Mayor’s ball?” and the reason is obvious ; Governor's
wives must entertain for State reasons, a great deal of
social flotsam, while private hostesses receive according to
their tastes and inclinations—savee?
There promises to be a large Cornstalk growth on the
Fleiningtoii lawn this race-meeting, judging by how
many dozens of people one meets who mean to take a
flight into Egypt with their very best clothes in a brand
new Gladstone-bag. All the new Pullman cars—the
excitement of the route this year—were filled up at an
early date, and would have been crowded over and over
again ; and the frenzy on the part of the Hot Tong to
strike Melbawne in time for the “ Darby ” (31st) supports
the well-known theory that the Cup isn’t in it for stylo
beside the better-dressed first day. Your Sappho has
packed up a few clean collars and a sharp pencil, and
hastens along with the great majority to shed the light of
her speaking countenance on the lawn and birdcage. I
mention this merely to explain why Sydney is going to
feel so desolate by and bye.
Yours affectionately,
SIPPHO SMITH.
A Newcastle correspondent states that
a very successful dental operation was per-
formed upon a leading local resident by Mr.
Charles 1. Harris, surgeon-dentist, of St. Janies’
Chambers, King and Macquarie streets, Sydney,
on Thursday last —the implantation of an a; tificial
tooth into the natural root being cleverly per-
formed. Dr. John Harris and Ur. W. Nixon
were present during the operation, and warmly
congratulated Mr. Harris on his success. Mr.
Harris, it will be remembered, was a partner of
Dr. Slate, of Macqnarie-street, but, on his separa-
tion from that gentleman, took rooms in the above
chambers, and lias furnished them throughout in
a most luxuriant manner, and fitted them
with all the modern improvements and require-
ments essential in the dental art for the higher
grades of his profession.

Our tile of the San Francisco Argonaut
was mislaid this mail, and when it came to hand
the contents seemed strangely familiar. Then we
remembered that we had read it all in the Sydney
Echo—except only the paragraph about Princess
Louise drinking brandy, and smoking cigars, and
dancing and whooping on the balcony of a hotel,
and things like that. The Echo had borrowed all
the rest, and reprinted it bodily.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea. or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade - ’
SO and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place ; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
Lord Hopetoun seems to be labouring
heavily under an impression that he is the inventor
of ensilage, and a sort of angel messenger to the
agricultural interests of Victoria. Anyway, he
invited a herd of farmers, fat politicians, free
lunchers and other representative persons to
Government House last week, to witness the con-
version into silage of a few oats and other vice-
regal green stuff. It was a ghastly exhibition of
pampered stupidity from first to last, aggravated
by the grovelling conduct of a lot of amateurs who
chucked their services about gratis, and fought
pitched battles in the background for the privi-
lege of forking tares atop of a waggon. But
nothing that transpired at the time was so naus-
eating as the Age report of next morning. The
free and independent radical journal rubbed its
nose in the dust and piped forth these glad
tidings—“A noteworthy incident of the proceed-
ings was that of Lord Hopetoun feeding the chaff-
cutter with his own hands.” Pagh ! Hopetoun
has been feeding The Bulletin chaff-cutter ever
since he made his triumphal entry into Melbourne
behind four dropsical steeds.
