SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Criterion “ Society.”
Theatre Royal “Robbery Under Arms.”
Her Majesty’s “The Dancing Girl."
Garrick “The Librarian.”
“ The Librarian,” George Leitch’s version
of Von Moser’s “Der Bibliotheka,” differs very
considerably in situation and incident from “The
Private Secretary,” though it is run on almost the
same lines. It is, however, more chaotic and
somewhat more improbable than the better-known
version. George Leiteh’s Robert Sparerib does
not suffer so badly from “ idfluedza ” as did
Thornton’s Spalding. His chief weakness is an
inordinate appetite for buns which he stores away
in a hat-box, and a womanish dread of dogs who
hunt him over walls and bite large slices of con-
fectionery out of his pockets. He walks as if on
stilts, and snorts mildly when pursued by the
malacca-cane of his peppery and alleged uncle.
This liver-cursed autocrat, noisy and not very
funny, in the hands of George Chalmers,
has considerably less to do than in Haw-
tree’s version ; nearly all his fat in the
“blot-yourself -out-scene” with the landlady
has been rendered down, till “ Uncle ” is
but a shadow of the athletic bully impersonated
by Harwood. Other characters have, however,
much the same value as in Hawtree’s version.
The spiritualistic aunt, played by Constance
Deorwyn, has a little more to do, and mesmerises
Leitch until he bursts forth into song. H. Diver
plays Gibson, _ the tailor, with a fine cockney
accent, but he is quite out of his element, and far
from inspiriting. The two voung ladies are
lumpishly dealt with by Nellie Lyons and L.
Clitherow ; Charles Holloway is all right as the
Squire, and Frank Cates is at home in his old
part. George Leitch’s version of Rudyard
Kipling’s novel, “The Light that Failed,” is in
active preparation.
** * *
Tom Robertson’s “Society” is possibly
new to Sydney because it contains eight scenes
and twenty-two characters, and gives an amount
of trouble in production that after-results scarcely
compensate for. To stage such a play at the
little Criterion, with its small stage and poor
storage-room, has been an experiment that only
very smart stage-management could have made
successful. “ Society ”is the tale of two wealthy
snobs, father and son, who want to become
fashionably known and imagine everything can be
done with a cheque-book, and a high-minded
nobleman, forced by his unhereditary generosity
to become a journalist. How the poor peer
eclipses the “brassy” boor, marries his girl, and
robs him of a seat in Parliament, need not be
recapitulated; is not the story known to every
reader of the London Journal? Illness having
taken Titheradge and Shine out of the bill after
Saturday night, Cosgrove has been playing Tom
Stylus, the impecunious pressman, fairly well on
short notice, though he might just as weil bo given
to understand that the ordinary pressman is not
likely to walk a drawing-room as if he’d just
stepped out of a stable-yard, and may be informed
that “ proofs ” are by no means narrow and abbre-
viated scraps of paper. Poulton successfully doubles
as Moses Aaron, the bailiff, and O'Sullivan, the
parliamentarian, made up to resemble Dan O’Con-
nor. Replacing Cosgrove, Brian England’s Smiffel
Lamb is about the best thing we’ve had from
him in Sydney. As Chodd, senior, Arthur Law-
rence is all right, and as the Ohoddy junior,
Boucicault scores heavily. Lyndal’s scribbling
scion of a noble house is, if anything, too lovely
and too good. Clitherow as the sleepy lord is not
particularly gratifying, nor does Marie Fraser
call for eulogy as the poor relation expected to
marry money, but Jenny Watt-Tanner’s Lady
Ptarmigant is a finished performance. So far,
“Society” has not knocked the public, and the
Criterion’s auditorium has usually a very gappy
appearance.
** * *
“The Daifcing Girl” is growing steadily
in favour at Sydney Her Majesty’s, but business
everywhere is dull and the treasury is never
apoplectically inclined. Viewed again one is in-
clined to think that this play is a good deal padded
out and that a heap of the allegory, like the bull-
dog, might be cut off without interfering Avith the
story. The manner in which Gooseberry plasters
his hair down on his forehead is aggravating, and
we are filled with an insane desire to heave bricks
at everyone who styles him “Your Grace.” As
that happens every two minutes and as we don’t
own a brickyard, this contract will never be
entered on.
*m * *
Nellie Farren, who came out for her
benefit in “ Cinder-Ellen ” to be covered with
flowers, has retired again to nurse her ankle. Her
under-study, Missßlenheim, has enough confidence
for a circus advance-agent, dances well, but can’t
sing, and is otherwise a caricature of the star.
I red. Leslie took his benefit in presence of a big
house, on Wednesday evening. Business has been
consistently good throughout the season, though
it dropped slightly in the d.c. when Nellie Farren
was out of the bill. As previously announced on
multifarious occasions Mme. Warskitt’s pupils
dance this (Thursday) evening, and Rolf Boldre-
wood gets a bold advertisement from Alfred
Dampier on Saturday night.
** * *
Up to date we have little news from Bondi,
except that last Sunday’s concert placed Alf.
Wyburd on a pinnacle up to which rival showmen
gaze, aided by telescopes, in goggle-eyed con-
fusion.
** * *
Last week Signor Guido Majeroni, actor,
started for a land where a few good men such as
he are doubtless much wanted. For, by its
own showing, the Salvation Band is going there,
and so is the Methodist Conference, and the
vacuous and vociferous street preacher on the keg,
and if they get there, then Majeroni’s pleasant
voice and noble countenance will do good service
by lending a little grace to the congregation. The
deceased Italian was a well - known figure on
Australian stage. He appeared first along
with Madame Ristori many years ago, and from
that time onwards he played a multitude of
characters. “ The Old Corporal ” was a part that
he made his own ; he was a brilliant success as
Rssex in “Queen Elizabeth,” and the other dramas
in which he scored heavily were legion. Financially,
hovVever, he was not much of a success. He never
appealed to the leg-show interest; he was too
refined for the great mass of the gallery crowd;
and the dress circle audiences praised him
rapturously—and stayed away because there was a
scarcity of tights in his dramas. The bald-headed
community passed on Majeroni : he was only a
cultured gentleman and a genius, and it wanted
songs and extremities. Majeroni died slowly of
consumption, and he was fast being forgotten
when his obituary notice in the daily papers
suddenly resurrected his memory. His remains
lie in the cemetery at Waverley (Sydney)—that
sandy waste which looks out on the sea—and as
he died poor, Daniel O’Connor made no speech
over his dust.
** * *
J. C. Williamson’s first appearance at
Melbourne Princess’ last Saturday caused a boom
house—the circle being very full indeed. J.C.’s
Kerry and Rip are just as fresh as on the day
they were made, and the double bill was from
start to finish an unmitigated triumph, in so far
as the star was concerned. The audience was
mostly that kind which goes into frantic mirth
over the presentment of a dull, fat man, who says
“Damn!” and mentions that he would like a
drink. Consequently, when Rip is very dry, the
circle howls with delight, and the stalls are one
great shriek. To us, however, there is nothing
so pathetic as the man who wants a drink. Last
Saturday we were almost moved to tears. What
fine, loveable old boozers are the sacred three —
Rip, Nick Vedder and Jacob Stein always
drunk if they can buy or cadge the where-
withal, and always content to allow their
wives to keep the pot boiling. Von Slaus in the
hands of Edwin Kelly is passable, and Nephew
Hector was fairly played by Charles Brown. Old
Harry Daniels represented Nick Vedder, and
C. Anderson, Jacob Stein. The parts of the
children, Hendrick Vedder and Meenie, were
nicely played by those two most tolerable speci-
mens of that awful infliction, the infant stage
prodigy, May Renno and Violet Ashton. Cautley
was the Hendrick Vedder of the third act, and
being only required to shiver his timbers and
wear his coat half way down his spine, was
extremely satisfactory. Maggie Knight as the
grown-up Meenie of the third act is anything but
pleasing, even though there is small scope for
acting in the part. As for Mrs. Edouin Bryer,
the Gretchen of the piece, she is absolutely
painful. There is no congruity in her representa-
tion of the shrewish vrow, and when she makes
three or four cat-jumps, in an endeavour to throw
herself into the arms of the newly-recognised Rip,
the people groan, and a cold shudder breaks out
all over the house. Her faint is built on entirely
original plans and specifications. She rises
majestically into the air to a height of quite six
inches from the ground, and having shot her-
self two feet forward, she goes astern, and
falls in a moaning heap. Her faint was
timed on Saturday, and it todk quite two
minutes by a fast watch. And under all
these circumstances, one wonders why Maggie
Moore (who was more the draw in “Struck Oil”
rr! all himself) did not enact the Gretchen.

The Italians at the Alexandra have signified
their intention of scooting at an early date, and
consequently the houses are improving. Thursday
and Friday, “ Lucrezia,” with Guidotti in the
name part, was put up with no small amount of
success. “ Traviata ” was repeated on Saturday,
and will again be played to-night (Thursday);
Melossi benefited on Tuesday with “Rigoletto,”
and had to hire two drays to carry his share of the
profits.
** * *
The music-halls fill to the doors when the
legitimate at the other houses is being played to
hard, unappreciative chairs. Snazelle opens next
Saturday at the Athenaeum with his nreviously-
mentioned version of “Enoch Arden.” Miss
Snazelle (says the advt.) will strike the piano keys
very hard several times, and Langdale, a gentle-
man who breathes gently into 12 feet of bamboo,
misnamed a bassoon, will likewise (lend the light
of his countenance to the show.
** * *
Geo. Rignold, at the Royal, continues to
fall into the tank with cheerful equanimity, and
the gallery begins to regard him as an obese seal
who may be expected to sit up and bark presently.
There is no mention of a change just yet, and it
is likely that the “Lights” will play over the Cup.

“The Yeomen of the Guard,” which was
staged for three nights at the Adelaide Royal, has
concluded the most prosperous comic opera season
ever held in that city, and the dollars of the devout
are consequently in the pocket of J. C. William-
son. Before leaving the city of godliness Elsie
Cameron recovered from influenza and was able to
warble successfully. Bracy also got back his voice.
Billy Elton suffered from hoarseness, but struggled
gamely on. Ryley, Vernon, Rosevear, Violet
Varley, Flora Graupner, and others have been in
rare voice. “The Jonquille,” by Guy Boothby,
a local author, will be revived for three nights for
his benefit prior to his departure for England,
where he goes in search of fame as a play-writer.
Maud Williamson plays the principal part in
Adelaide. Fitzgerald’s Circus has been attracting
enormous crowds within their spacious tent, and
Brofessor Anderson has likewise been doing well
at Garner’s Rooms.
** * m
Miss Myra Kemble and Co. knocked out
“Jane” in three rounds at the Brisbane Royal
last week. Inside and outside the prospect has
been cheerless. The descendant of the Kembles
filled the title part, and the others hung about
anyhow, like the ghost of a vanished beer, or the
memory of the cigar that was smoked in the long
ago. The piece floundered heavily from act to
act and seemed to Vish it was dead, and among
the minor members of the company there was
visible a weary longing to throw the whole con-
tract up. Whatever fun cropped out was mostly
due to the authors of the play, although Miss
Kemble and Mr. Lester did their best to give the
S reduction a show. In the same bill, “ The Cape
fail,” a one-act play by Clement Scott, was
perpetrated.

A Melbourne paper says: ‘‘Madame Melba has had a
singular adventure at Paris. She had been engaged to
take part in the musical portion of a fashionable wedding,
but the Archbishop objected to ladies singing in churches.
Tnen the lady in charge of the proceedings induced
Madame Melba to hide behind the organ, and put a
lad, missal in hand, to stand up in the choir and pretend
to sing, while the prima donna poured forth her wealth of
voice. The Bulletin here draws a fancy picture of a
piuua donna hidden in a doubled-up condition in some
dusty recess among the rats, and lying low till her time
ca , , . ne .. t 9 Wouldn t it have been a good deal brighter
a he if it had been pointed put that the boy was Melba in
disguise, and that she had arrived carrying her clothes in
a bundle, and dressed herself secretly under a seat ?

One drawback to belonging to the stage must be having
your tea so early-about five p.m. It looks really
sheepish when you pass the suburban dwelling-place of
that sparkling comedy company to see the heroes and
heroines of theevening’shighfalutin’sittinground the table
sipping tea, with the sun in the heavens and a few brats
looking through the palings. It doesn’t chime in at all
with one’s pre-conoeived. notions of the whirling, riotous,
Bohemian existence the glittering actor-man is supposed
to lead, when you see him carving the mutton at 4.30,
while the juvenile lead with the wicked eyes takes a
second cup of tea—it looks from the outside as if the
whole gang had been sent back to the nursery. Of course
histrions must let their victuals shake down before the
curtain rises or else their utterances would sound fat
and over-fed, and the jeune premier would probably
hiccough during his declaration of love—but there is no
occasion to make your modest meals with doors and
windows wide open to the public gaze. And when people
look in upon this inntcent spectacle in the afternoon and
then go and see the play at night, they can’t forget the
happy family aspect the company presented strung round
the early teapot, and the glamour of the show grows plain
and ordinary, and coldly material in consequence.

General and Mrs. Mite, the pair who look like our
esteemed grandparents viewed through the wrong end of

going to plunge on Correze.
In one of Rider Haggard’s stories a shipw'recked mil-
lionaire, dying on an island where paper and ink are non-
existent, has his will executed on a girl’s back with a fish-
bone. It nearly kills the girl, but the will holds water.
A play based on this tale is to be produced shortly in New
York, and now the trouble is that there is an awful
amount of writing to go on the heroine’s shoulders, and
the lady who takes the part is so small that there isn’t
room for it.
British theatre-goers never take to plays on Joan of
Arc because J. Bull’s instinct scouts the idea of Divine
agencies interfering to promote the defeat and humilia-
tion of England.

Theatrical business at Hobart is dreadfully handicapped
by the only theatre being situated right away in the
suburbs. Alanagers who know the value of casual “ biz.”
will understand what a serious thing this must be. In
consequence of the recent bank smash land could be pur-
chased at reasonable rates almost anywhere in Hobart.
The old Bank of V.D.L. itself could be converted into an
opera house for a song.
Charles Jamrach, the lately-deceased London naturalist
and showman, was thrice married, and the last of his
wives survives him. On the death of the second a friend
came to condole with him. “ Yes,” said Jamrach, “ she
was a good wife, but she never took kindly to the animals,
and even in the winter she would never let the snakes
sleep under the bed.”

away from the right place. Yet if he struck the correct
spot the audience would probably howl at him in derision.”

Johnny L. Hall, son of the late lamented comedian,
gave up the professional stage some time back, but
occasionally buys a cheap stick of grease-paint for use
at amateur theatricals. His latest show was on Oct. 14,
at North Alelbourne, when he held up his own end of the
log and most of the middle as the low comedy man in
“ Daddy Hardacre,” and the bailiff in “In Possession.”
The young J.L. plainly shows his descent from the
inimitable Captain Gingah, although he has not got the
experience of stage and humans which the elder
possessed.

According to Molesworth Greene, a Border
squatter who owns stations in Q., N.S.W., and
S.A., the rabbits are like an innumerable be-
leaguering army trying to penetrate from N.S.W.
into Q. “They have made a broad, hard road
along the N.S.W. side of the (rabbit-proof) fence,
and on this they tramp up and down, trying to
get through. The country on the Queensland side
(of the fence) is luxuriant with vegetation after
this fine season, while on the New South Wales
side of the line it is a desert of red sand, relieved
only by rabbits and black stumps of dead bushes.”
Last week a vague report got somehow
into circulation in London that Queen Victoria
was dead. It was promptly contradicted, and
therefore it seems probable at the time of writing
that she is still alive. But if she had been dead
the statement would have been contradicted all
the same. For some inscrutable reason when a
royalty is taken seriously bad the one prevailing
idea is to keep the fact dark ; when a royalty
is dead the great objeet is to keep that dark
also. Albert the Good had become a remains
some time before the doctors gave up publishing
statements about his rapid recovery, and he
wasn’t an aggravated case of the kind by any
means. More than once in history a dead mon-
arch has run an empire for three weeks, and no
one ever noticed much difference, sometimes the
deceased potentate seemed to manage things a
little better than the live one, but that was all.
Joe Jinks alias Joseph Jackson the other
day escaped from two policemen while being con-
veyed to the Penrith (N.S.W.) lock-up, was
speedily recaptured, immediately taken back to
the courthouse, and given an additional three
months. For what offence ? He did not “ break
gaol; ”he simply scooted for liberty in the street
and ran an impromptu footrace with the police.
If two constables couldn’t keep him in safe
custody they should get three months and not (as
an Australian Judge once ruled) the prisoner, who
merely followed a natural inclination for freedom
as soon as an opportunity presented itself. It
would be interesting to know under what statute
the Penrith Bench gave Jinks the extra three
months? Or was it merely for “contempt” in
not obeying the previous judgment of their
Worships? The writer knew a man who escaped
from the Port Curtis lock-up, in Queensland,
years ago, by rushing out when the keeper
opened his cell-door. He, too, was speedily re-
captured—fell down a saw-pit, if memory serves
aright, with the hue-and-cry man scrunch atop
of him—and it was ruled that the police could
formulate no valid charge against him, since he
only ran out of an open door and did not legally
“ break out of gaol.”

fashionable crime, the influenza has become the
most popular affliction, and eucalyptic remedies
are the most reliable specifics within easy reach of
the common sneezer who can’t afford to worry a
guinea-a-time doctor, but who must get well' on
the cheap, if at all. Among the various eucalyptic
oils, “ essences,” “ spirits,” &c., Nance’s Mia-Mia
Eucalypti Extract stands highly recommended, as
being pure, strong, and certainly curative. The
efficacy of the aromatic, volatile principle of the
Australian “ blue-gum ” (eucalyptus globulus) for
all ailments of the respiratory system has now
been established for more than a-qnarter of a
century. Nance’s Mia-Mia Extract is manufac-
tured in Sandhurst, Vic., the Sydney depot being
at 480 Oxford-street, Paddington.*

feated for a Sydney constituency, the old man
said : —“The door of my political life is closed for

Hon. W. H. Reynolds, speaking on Sir
Geo. Grey’s Law Practitioners Bill, in the Maori-
land Upper House : “ If I had my way I would
hang one half the existing lawyers and quarter
the other half.”
It is going to be enacted in N.S. Wales
that for the future a camel is to be an animal.
This was rendered necessary through a Bourke
bench of magistrates deciding some time ago that
it wasn’t one.
G. D. Carter, M.P., Vic., says: “ If Mr.
Munro complains of himself for voting 25 times
at an election, why not bring in a Bill to dis-
qualify himself, instead of us, who only want to
vote twice ?”
That “sardine-tin,” the gunboat Tweed,
has been sunk at Hongkong. She was one of a
dozen failures, unable to steam, steer, sail, or
fight, built in 77. The balance are rusting out in
the dockyards.
Out of the ten members of tire incoming
N.S.W. Ministry four are lawyers. If G. R.
Dibbs means to be a success he will chuck three
of them out, and keep his eye on the other one.
Four lawyers is a ruinous percentage.
Morehead, M.L.A. (Q-), would like to
disfranchise the Civil Service. It helped to fling
him out of the Val'ey representation not long ago,
and Morehead has a long memory, and a fathom-
less smile, and a dark and vengeful soul.
Some iconoclast proposed in the Victorian
Upper House that in future a Judge should be
merely an ordinary bald-headed citizen, and that
his horse-hair top-knot should be abolished. The
ancient decoration was saved by only one vote.

at the Queensland Government Labour Bureau in
six days. Yet the blind and crippled Coalition
spends £32,000 a year of the people’s cash to bring

“It is no new plan, this of ‘General’
Booth’s. Moses led forth the people of Israel
under God’s own guidance, in the days of old, to
try a new country ; but it was not a success. I
don’t say tiiat God couldn’t do it, but He didn’t.”

moments filled up with its friends, supporters,

was beginning to slide into the abyss he tried to
square the Labour-party with the offer of two
billets on the Public Works Committee. The
bribe wasn’t big enough. If he had offered his
scalp it might have been a more solid inducement.
Parkes held office in N.S.W. nearly twice
as long as “ Slippery Charlie ” Cowper. Parkes
was in office 14 years, 5 months, (i days ; Cowper,
7 years, 8 months, 22 days ; and Martin, 5 years,
5 months, 25 days. Parkes drew £28,800 13s. 4d.
from the Treasury in official screw. Where is dot
monish now?
Mr. W. P. Crick is said to contemplate
retirihg from West Macquarie to oppose Mr.
Barton at East Sydney. Should he do so, he has
a fair chance of being left. The man who beats
Barton has a big contract. That politician’s
affable smile alone would carry him through, and
Crick can’t smile worth a hang.
A conservative newspaper is not a pro-
fitable spec, in Maoriland. One daily, run in this
interest, has shown a debit balance for the past 10
years averaging about £5OOO per annum. A big
firm of solicitors subsidise the company to the
extent of £lOOO a year, which is deducted pro rata
from the rents collected by them for about a dozen
absentee landlords.
“Oxley” Grimes, M.L.A., Queensland:
“I believe it would be to the advantage of hon.
members and of the good government of the
colony, if the bar in the parliamentary refresh-
ment room were closed while the House is sitting.
If hon, members do not come into the House
absolutely drunk, they are often talkatively
drunk. It is often the whisky, and not the man,
that speaks.”
A British voter got disqualified recently
as follows :—For six years he had been lodging in
a back room and had voted as an elector for
Chelsea. Some weeks ago he removed into the
front room and was knocked off the roll, because
it transpired that though the back room was in
Chelsea the front one—which opened off it —was
in Westminster. The question now arises—what
would happen if he lived in the doorway ?

HE : “ Ah, ray dear, you will never let me finish a sentence. I was about to say sherry—
and bitters.”
Sandford, newly elected labor-member for
Christchurch. Maoriland, is a comp, in the
Lyttelton Times Office. When the result of the
polling was announced, giving him a nearly 2 to 1
majority over the conservative candidate, Sand-
ford straightway bolted for the office and went
back to his frame. The enthusiastic crowd that
wanted to whoop with him and liquor him up
never thought of looking for him there, so it was a
lost celebration.
The people of Switzerland have decided by
a crushing majority for Protection, for a State
bank, and for making the issue of bank-notes
Government business only. This is solid demo-
cracy, and it has been accomplished without any
long-haired politician stumping the country and
getting up a “campaign;” without any crisis;
and with no speeches worth mentioning. The
Referendum works silently and without any fuss,
and it records the absolute will of the people as
nothing else can.
The reprobate who knocked a sky-pole
through the roof of the Victorian Constitution by
prigging the Speaker’s mace is still untracked.
Meanwhile, Parliament crowds through with an
old second-hand bauble, which had been stowed
away for years in a lumber-room. Sir Matt.
Davies has now, wo understand, ordered a new
mace, to be cut according to the latest fashion
decollete and pouffe, and he is going to sleep on top
of it for the future, and hold it in his hand when
he is under the shower-bath.
Is the Mr. Curnow, appointed to a
clerical position by the N.S.W. Chicago Com-
mission, a relation of Editor Curnow of the
S.M. Herald, and of ex-Minister Brunker? A
nephew of Mr. Brunker named Curnow was
appointed as a shorthand writer once to a royal
commission ; but it turned out that he did not
write shorthand —at all events not rapidly enough
to fill the billet. Editor Curnow is a brother-in-
law of ex-Minister Brunker. Which explains
many things.
Johnston, M.L.A., declares that the
Labour-party will require democratic concessions
from Premier Dibbs, and will not support Dibbs’
Protectionist policy in return. Therefore, suppos-
ing—as seems to be assumed —that Dibbs isn’t a
democrat by instinct any more than Parkes, and
is only giving concessions in return for support,
and considering that Johnston practically informs
him in advance that he won’t get the support
even after he has given the concessions—isn’t
there a fair chance that Dibbs may rise up and
state that the bargain is too one-sided altogether ?
Another Indian native journalist has got
into trouble. Like the editor of the Bangajsasi,
he had been easting reflections on his gracious
Sovereign, and was arrested. But as the charge
of sedition against the previous editor fell
through, and as this case was even weaker than
the other one, the second pressman wasn’t brought
to trial. He was simply kicked a little by the
police during his arrest, and judiciously knocked
against the wall, and as he died an hour after-
wards much beastly trouble was thereby saved.
Salomons, the Dibbs Government’s repre-
sentative in the Upper House, states —according
to the Sydney D.T.—that he wants “ to conserve
the freedom and liberty of the individual and
uphold the rights of property, in so far as they
were provided for and in accordance with that
political and social constitution which was one of
the greatest inheritances from the mother country,
and which had been slowly built up during hun-
dreds of years, by some of the greatest minds of
the various generations.” If Salomons is going to
fire off great slabs like this at short intervals he
will be a ghastly incubus on the new Cabinet.
J. Macfarlane, M.L.A. (Q.), in replying to
the debate on his motion to prohibit drinking at
the Parliamentary bar during session, graphically
described a scene that occurred ten years ago in the
Bananaland Assembly, when a dead-drunk mem-
ber was painted as a clown. Macfarlane’s yarn
recalls, an occasion on which the razzle-dazsdi
extended to the Press gallery and attacked the
sacred person of a city editor, who boasted the
possession of a magnificent carroty beard. Some
legislative joker, finding the Archimedean lever-
man asleep, deftly cut off one-half of his only
ornament.
Boyd Dunlop Morehead the other night
wasted 40 minutes of the Queensland Parliament’s
time in baiting Speaker Norton for having ordered
the closure of “ the main door in the new wing”
of Parliament House. B.D.M. ac-
cused Norton of assuming “a Balmaceda-like
dictatorship,” the grievance appearing to be that
hon. members couldn’t get at the hat-rack—they
don’t wear “ Andykellies ” that can be doubled up
and poked in a pocket up there—without “ walk-
ing half-way round the building.” This reminds
us of the time when Bent complained that the
fence round the Victorian Parliament House was
so high that he couldn’t get back after dinner.
It is a serious sign that the times are out
of joint when a London financial paper begins to
talk like this :
We very much doubt if Russia will get through the
coming winter without seeing the assassination of the
Czar and a Republic declared. And if so, Germany, ruled
to-day by a young prig, will follow suit. And let these
two countries decide for the new regime, and bang goes
the lot. Although we all seem to believe that England is
monarchical, do not let us hug the idea too closely to
our bosoms. The Queen has scandalised the nation with
the berths and salaries given to these German Tecks, or
Christians, or Battenbergs, and any fine day we may see
the whole system of government changed. Well, what
will it matter to the crowd? Victoria has “absorbed”
(good word, that) as much cash as she can ever spend,
and if the Prince of Wales has chosen to discount an
inheritance he may never get, it is his own look-out.
Inspired writers in the Queensland press
are endeavouring to reconcile the white workers to
the wholesale Italian immigration scheme tendered
by the Griffith Government as a sop to the sugar-
planters. These champions of mean whites assert
that the coming Italian is a desirable importation,
and that each of them will be required to produce,
in evidence of his hona-hdes and sterling value :
1, A certificate of good conduct (from whom?);
2, a certificate declaring him to be a hona fide
agricultural labourer (probably from a gaoler, or
gamekeeper, or wharf-loafer); 3, a police certifi-
cate of ignorance as to his character, or a certifi-
cate supposed to be from a policeman, more or
less; 4, a medical certificate (same old guinea
deception proving that twenty-four of him ran
past a doctor in ten seconds without showing
any disease); 5, a passport. All of which is very
comforting indeed.
Why didn’t Dibbs appoint Crick as
Government representative in the Upper House?
Dibbs has strong democratic tendencies, and—
unless his principles go the way MTlwraith’s did—
he must tackle the question of reforming the
Council some day or other. Unluckily, the Coun-
cil can’t be abolished off-hand in a legal way,
because it must pass the Bill for its own abolition,
and it won’t do that; and it is difficult to flood it
with a democratic majority, for the average demo-
crat goes to seed almost as soon as he gets into
that Chamber, and becomes petrified. Therefore,
the shortest wav would be to send Crick there and
let him talk the Council to death. Besides, a
Government is no good unless it has some humour
in it, and the spectacle of the excited little man
plunging round in that solemn assemblage, with
his hair standing up and his coat-tails flying in six
different directions, and his collar and tie bursting
from sheer frenzy, while the old fat men sat
around in horror, would be a gladsome thing and
calculated to elevate the soul.
The London Court Journal says :— “Mr.
Gladstone dines out with a regularity and daring
that could not be excelled by a man of half his
years. One who meets him often at these gas-
tronomic tournaments say that Mr. G.’s participa-
tion in their enjoyments is by no means per-
functory. Some men past (50, still dining out,
enjoy themselves under severe restrictions, daintily
picking their way through the menu. Mr. G.,
past 80, is apparently under no such restrictions.
He eats what comes, generally right thi’ough'the
menu, and can take his glass of port after his
libation of champagne, a test from which many an
ardent diner-out would shrink. His mental
activity is on a par with his physical vigor. His
conversation is a never flagging flood, brimful of
interest. If it were only possible to reproduce a
verbatim report of his conversation at a dinner-
party, where the personal surroundings are suf-
ficiently interesting to excite him to talk, there
would be produced a volume of interest, biographi-
cal, autobiographical, and historical. His memory
is marvellous, and his power of graphically recall-
ing an incident is the admiration of even the
gentlemen behind the chairs.” Mr. Gladstone is
to be congratulated on possessing a perfect liver,
not all men can say the same; the slightest over
indulgence in eating or drinking is sufficient with
some people to upset the liver and cause bilious
attacks ; these eventually lead to serious disease
and make the sufferer’s life one continual misery.
Timely use of Clements Tonic with an occasional
dose of Dr. Fletcher's pills will always cure the
most serious cases, as shown by the proofs vouch-
safed by Mr. W. Kelly, Albury, N.S.W., who
says: “For nearly twelve months I have been
subjected to such a most distressing disease, that
I thought my constitution was breaking up. I
had most acute pains in my chest, and my liver
was so enlarged that I could not lie on my right
side, there were also sharp pains around the
kidneys combined with severe headaches simply
unbearable, causing great physical weakness. I
was treated by doctors but only experienced
temporary relief from my complicated disorders.
When my condition was most critical I was per-
suaded to try Clements Tonic, which I took with
Dr. Fletcher’s pills, and after the use of the first
boctle of Clements Tonic my pains vanished like
magic. I used in all three bottles of Clements
Tonic and two boxes of Fletcher’s Fills, and the
swelling of my liver was removed. I have gained
greatly in weight and my chest and lungs are
stronger than over. lam well known as an old
resident and my recovery has excited much
interest here.”

When the Newgate chaplain went to see
Forger Fauntleroy an hour before the time fixed
for his hanging, he was airing his clean shirt at
the fire.
By the death of Mr, William Byrnes,
G. H. Cox becomes “father” of the N.S.W.
Legislative Council, having been appointed in
June 1863.
John Williams, for 32 years Crown-solicitor
of N.S. Wales, was buried at Rookwood on the
21st. Mr. Williams’ predecessor—Mr. W. W.
Billyard—is still living.
Harry Stockdale, of buffalo - syndicate
notoriety, is now busily engaged in chasing out-
side buffalo-hunters off his big lease of country
around Alligator River, N.T.
By the “ smash ” of an old-established
flour-mill down South, Hugh Murdoch, one of the
heaviest losers in the V.D.L. Bank, will be let in
to the tune of £13,000. It never rains but it
pours.
The “ father ” of the N.S.W, Legislative
Council, Mr. William Byrnes, a native of Sydney,
died at Parramatta, last Sunday, aged 84. He
was for 34 years a member of the somnolent
chamber.
If the late John Williams, Crown Solicitor,
had belonged to the horse-hair section of the law
he would have died Chief Justice of N.S. Wales.
The advantages of hair over brains are thus once
more demonstrated.
Jules Verne began life as a lawyer. He
couldn’t even defend a common drunk worth a
cent, however, so he gave it up. Some of the
people who got into gaol through being defended
by Verne are there yet.

A plausible pieman named Thomas Skif-
fins has just been sentenced at Rockhampton, Q.,
to “ three years hard.” He had a balance of
6s. Id. way back in a Barcaldine bank, and passed
cheques to the extent of £lOl, pleading that he
had no idea he had “ overdrawn.”
“The sins of the father,” &c. There is
now no longer any reason to speculate as to the
cause of the Austrian Crown Prince’s suicide. He
had formed a liaison with a lady who was the
illegitimate daughter of his own Imperial father.
When the couple discovered this, they agreed to
die together.
Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C. (S.A.), who
a few days ago said he would “shoot the first
man who called him a Republican,” displayed his
true measure of pluck last week by piteously
claiming the protection of Parliament against a
fellow-member —Kirkpatrick—who attacked him
in the press !
Mrs, Dibbs, mother of Premier G. R.
Dibbs, of Mr, T. R. Dibbs of the Commercial
Bank, and of Mr. John 0. Dibbs, died at North
Sydney on the 21st, aged 82 years. She was a
very old colonist and a lady who was universally
esteemed. She leaves 20 grand-children and 12
great-grand-children.
Someone writes from England to the
daily papers that he is tearfully surprised to find
our gracious monarch such a small, thick and
dowdy person, in comparison with her Sydney
statue. The fact is, she would have been just
like the statue, only Providence used over much
of her material for circumference.
A Warrnambool (Vic.) man, being sued
for an account by a local chemist, swore in Court
that the plaintiff’s “ liver tonic ” was nothing but
pure whisky, sold almost openly over the counter.
He got verdicted against, and is to be prosecuted
for perjury. Thus happeneth it to the recreant
who roundeth on his private tap.
According to Pavkes, the doctor says that
all his organs are sound except his heart. Where
did that doctor look for Parkes’s heart ? If he
explored in the usual place then he might as well
have expected to find the old man’s liver in his
boots. Did it ever once dawn on him to hunt in
the patient’s pocket, for instance ?
Phil. May recently felt a longing to
draw Parkes once more for the sake of old associa-
tions, so he made a picture of the ancient one for
London Pick-me-up. And then the ignorant pro-
prietor labelled it “ Sir Henry Parker,” and Phil,
realised when he saw it that life was empty, and
everything was a hollow unreality.

for him, for many of the German people are

Behold, this is Tummy :—Two Princes (of
Wales and of Saxony); three Dukes (of Cornwall,
of Rothesay, and of Saxe-Coburg-Gotha); three
Earls (of Chester, of Garrick, and of Dublin); one
Baron (of Renfrew); and Lord of the Isles of
Scotland; also two Privy Councillors, eight
Knights, and a tremendous variety of foreign
noblemen.
Poor old Frank Dunnett, well known in
old days in the Hobart Survey Office, had his
little all in the smashed-up Bank of V.D.L. The
crash killed him, for he quietly coiled up and died,
his last words being “ The Bank ! the Bank !
the Bank ! ” This is a pleasant reflection for some
people, and should lie heavy on many a moral
financier’s chest.
It is alleged that at Aix the Princess
Louise, Marchioness of Lome, was lately seen
imbibing brandy-and-soda and smoking cigars on
the balcony of the Casino. One evening recently
her highness executed a Highland dance, with all
its appropriate stamps and shouts, for the delecta-
tion of her own circle and incidentally for the joy
of many profane outsiders.
Mr. Pilcher, a'banker in Perth (W.A.),
has given up the overdraft business to join the
Church. We remember another bank-manager,
at Orange (N.S.W.), who became so godly that he
would not handle the money lodged by his
publican customers, but 'delegated the duty to an
assistant. He left the bank and went preaching,
but becoming “eccentric,” had to be sent to a
lunatic asylum.
Between 1835 and 1856 thirty-nine Angli-
can bishops died in possession of personal
property, exclusive of all real estate, varying in
amount from £12,000 to £140,000, the average
being about £54,000 each, and their total per-
sonalty over £2,000,000 sterling. The average
bishop doesn’t believe in a heaven evidently. If
he did he wouldn’t scramble so hard after the coin
of this brief earth.
Thus M. Leon Delbos, in his book
“ Pauvre Humanite,” on Salvationists and their
“ General ”;—
Vice and all the low instincts of the beast are written
on their countenances, which often have nothing human
about them. . . . Look at the head of their founder !
Have you ever seen anything more ignoble, more
diabolical ? The head is that of a bird of prey, the hands
are the claws of a vulture, the slender body that of a
s erpent, the eye is lewd and cruel.

Thistlewaite, prominent Melbournian, who
died last week, was Mayor of the model and
moral borough, Emerald Hill, when the Duke of
Edinburgh first visited Victoria, and it was he
who prayed that canny potentate to “bless” his
municipality with a visit. That so genuine a toady
was permitted to live for 24 years afterwards is a
strong argument for believing that the Archangels
don’t hanker after the society of flunkeys.
E. Cobcroft, aged 75 years, who retired
from the Forest Rangership of Singleton (N.S.W.)
District, on 19th December, ’9O, with a gratuity
of £125, has just been re-gazetted. He happens
to be father-in-law of Attorney-General Simpson.
The man who succeeded him in Singleton was
named John Smith and he happens to be brother
of the present Minister for Mines. Government
officials will somehow persist in being relatives of
prominent politicians.
A good old sport weighed out at Dubbo
(N.S.W.) on 20th Oct., when W. G. Orbell, host
of the Royal Hotel there, died. Those who were
travellers over the mountains in the early fifties
will remember him as a slashing driver, one of the
best and most daring whips that ever handled a
team. He left the roads in ’GO, and developed as
a publican in Hartley; afterwards ran another pub.
at Mount Victoria; and in 72 settled down in
Dubbo. He was about 55 years of age.
Mr, William Wilkin, formerly Under-
secretary for Public Instruction (N.S.W.), has
been appointed private secretary to the Executive
Commission to the Chicago Exhibition. This
gentleman has the good luck to bo pitchforked
into quite a lot of little billets ; but did not The
Bulletin understand that he retired from the
U.S.-ship because he had some incurable com-
plaint, which could only be alleviated by a large
pension. Where is that complaint now ?
Rudyard Kipling’s brother tills a fruit-
garden and makes jam (which he presents to the
neighbouring women), and lives the life of a
solitary bachelor in Northern Queensland. By
the way, an uncle of Kipling’s was a Methodist
lay-preacher and bootmaker in Melville-street,
Hobart Town in the fifties. His preaching wasn’t
very brilliant—whether his boots were brilliant or
not we haven’t heard. It is a tough contract to
be a great man’s relative, and there are few who
are equal to the job.
Mr. Tom Finney, of Brisbane—who was
incidentally alluded to in The BpLLKTiN as a
Roman Catholic —writes to state that he isn’t one,
but belongs to the Church of England. As a
rule, when The Bulletin says a man is a Roman
Catholic he has got to remain one, but, considering
all the circumstances of the case, Mr. Finney will
be let off this time. He may belong to the
Church of England, therefore, if he feels that
way. At the same time he is warned not to do
this sort of thing too frequently.
When the Irish collected £30,000 to free
Parnell’s estate from mortgage, the latter con-
sulted the O’Gorman Mahon as to whether he
should accept the money, adding that the O’G.
had better think over the matter for a day.
“Divil a think,” replied the O’Gorman Mahon.
“Oi decide at wanst that ye take the coin, so
that whin the Tipperary and Kilkenny people are
peltin’ yez wid shtones and mud a couple of years
from now ye can turn round and slap your pockets
at the blaggards.” The O’Gorman Mahon knew
mankind.
Sir Graham Berry seeks to excuse his
possession of the gewgaw of knighthood by the
assertion that he accepted the title “ as a public
dooty ” and an aid to the proper recognition of his
status among Australian Agents-General. A
hollow gag, this, which might be used to excuse
any defilement or grovelling. Doubtless, Berry
would accept a dukedom, or even the Imperial
Crown of Australia “as a public dooty.” Berry
is supposed to have his bright blue eye onColling-
wood, which seems to suggest that his title hasn’t
helped his intelligence much.
Governor Norman, cf Queensland, is a
modest, afternoon-tea sort of being, who looks
more like a meek and trodden-on curate than a
General. The Guv’mint ’Ouse crowd evidently
bores him, for he roams about the suburbs in a
solitary, unprotected way, or propels a canoe along
the river. He travels like an ordinary being in
an ordinary train, and does his best to offend no
one and to keep dark. But if he doesn’t stop
roaming about he’ll get lost one of these days, and
then some of the unemployed will find him and
claim a heavy reward for bringing him home.
Bananaland Legislative Assembly has
agreed to a vote of £2OOO, to be placed in trust
for the widow and family of the late J. M.
Macrossan, once the leader of the N.Q. Northern
Party and the legislative champion of miners. An
equal amount has already been raised by private
subscription. During his lifetime, Macrossan was
popularly credited with having feathered his nest
• —malicious people even went the length of hint-
ing at partnerships with public contractors, while
he was Minister for Works, but Jack successfully
lived down that scandal. Later on, when there
were many strange dealings with Mount Morgan
scrip in high quarters it was a popular fallacy that
Macrossan was “in it” and had made his pile.
But he was too honest to make his pile anywhere,
and he died poor.
M. Jules Gros, a composer of music-hall
songs, who died recently in Paris, once founded a
funny Republic—the Republic of Coumani—on a
tract of no-man’s-land, on the northern frontier of
Brazil, inhabited by Indians. He might have
founded a dynasty in those South American
forests, and lived and died President of Coumani,
but, sighing for fame, he established himself in
Paris and created a “Cabinet Council,” whose
chief business seemed to be to drink absinthe, and
to distribute the “ Order of the Star of Coumani ”
pretty promiscuously. France and Brazil repudi-
ated his claims, and the Government of British
Guiana politely advised him not to insist on
passing through its territory cn route to his
“republic.” So he returned to Paris and main-
tained himself by composing music and selling
“ the star ” till he died.
The Enfranchisement of Woman.
The leaders in office all over the world, and
especially in this continent, are proposing that
woman, or at least some of her, should have the
suffrage extended to her. Very well then. This
action is partly the inevitable answer that must
be made to the voice of the people, especially as
election times are approaching, and partly an
attempt to bolster up the iniquitous system of
plural voting which has received such a deatli blow
from the recent accession of workers’ represen-
tatives to Australian Parliaments. Whether or
not the departure will prove productive of gcod or
ill, whether the enfranchised woman will ncgleit
her house duties and vote for the ornamental
candidate with the curly golden beard to the
exclusion of the rugged Hampden, or the patriot
with odd eyes, is a matter that rests in the womb
of the future. Woman’s political enfranchise-
ment by the march of progress may be an umnixed
blessing or an undoubted curse. Woman’s dis-
enfranchisement from disease by the sterling
virtues of Warner's safe cure can be nothing but a
heaven-sent boon. The great number of com-
plaints from which our mothers, the past builders
of the world, and our wives and sisters, who are
to be the mothers of the next generation suffer,
vanish before the conquering remedies of H. H.
Warner & Co. Muscular weakness, tumors,
abscesses, and all the nameless ills known
generally as female complaints are evidences of
blood corruption, caused by the duties of
maternity, or the errors of dress, diet, and habits.
It is the special duty of the kidneys to keep the
blood pure and capable of doing its work, and
when tliis function is impaired only Warner’s
safe cure can restore it. Patients may reasonably
expect great results from the use of one or two
bottles, but four or six bottles are necessary in
some cases to produce permanent results. But
the results are so thorough that not one of the
thousands of Australian women thus restored to
the enjoyment of health would regret the use of
even ten times the quantity mentioned of the
famous safe cure were such a quantity necessary.

Society
The monotony of Hobart life has just been
very much enlivened by an elopement, the
naughty ones being a scion of a bold clan,
formerly resident in the New Norfolk district,
and the dashing eldest daughter of a well-known
boarding-house proprietress in the solemn city.
The young lady had been locked up for a fort-
night on bread and water, quite in the old sweet
way, but managed to get out through the venti-
lator and join her tawny-moustached lover, who
was waiting on the ash-heap below, and, mounting
a parcels-delivery cart, they made for the steam-
boat and fled for Sydney, never more to part.
# * *
lb is a wise preacher who knows his own
theology but a still wiser one who says nothing
about it.
# * *
The marriage of Miss Annie Jeannette
Menzies, daughter of the late well-known hotel
proprietor in Melbourne, to Mr. Bertie Lyndsay
Hopkins, R.N., took place recently in London,
and was duly announced with a flourish of trum-
pets and a long list of guests in the pictorial
society papers. It was, in reality, a shoddy affair,
and the P. and O. and other merchant services
must have been raked up for men, judging by the
list of invited. The bride’s mother gave a big
blow-out, after the ceremony, at her house in
Devonshire Place. When the happy couple left
for the honeymoon they were accompanied to the
railway-station by nearly all the guests, who made
quite a sensation on the platform, throwing rice
and old shoes after the train. Someone tied an
old boot to the handle of the carriage, which
seemed to afford much amusement to the non-
descript crowd of idlers which collected to witness
the unwonted sight.
* # *
Something serious is going to come about.
A member of the Bulletin staff dreamt that
Queen Victoria was dead, and that the Sydney
D.T. published eight columns stating that it was
very glad she w'as gone.
Also, as a further sign of the times, we
dreamt last night that Parkes was deceased. It
was a weird and uncomfortable dream, for the old
man got square in his last moments by dying in
The Bulletin office, and his remains were in the
editor’s room. Then we called round on
Dan. O’Connor and Abigail and B. R.
Wise, and suggested that they should arrange
about the funeral, but they declined unani-
mously. Thereupon we decided to look
after it ourselves. We embezzled a cart some-
where and hired a lame black horse, and the
coffin was made in the dead of night in The
Bolletin cellar. The business manager disguised
himself in a slouch hat and a long murky cloak
and a dark lantern, and went out secretly at mid-
night with a muffled tread, and borrowed a big
tombstone from a stone-cutter’s yard in Castle-
reagh-street, Sydney. He nearly broke his back
getting it down as far as the Herald, and then
he hired a coffee-stall on wheels, and the granite
slab was put into it and run down to its destina-
tion. When it arrived there the coffee-stall man
was assassinated and buried in the cellar so
that he might not betray the secret, and his vehicle
was jerked into the harbour. These preliminaries
being completed, the deceased was conveyed into
the cart—he seemed to weigh as much as a
mountain of lead, and the whole staff was worn to
rags lifting him—and the coffin and the tombstone
were put in after him, and the black horse started
solemnly down a long black road that was lined
with poems and I O U’s. A skeleton man riding
on a skeleton mule went on in front, ringing a
dinner-bell, and in this order the procession
went straight ahead for eighteen years, through
a land where everything was black. Now
and then it stopped for refreshments, and
the refreshments were black also. Finally,
just as the funeral was getting well into its nine-
teenth year, and was still going, the horse sud-
denly turned round and asked if Parkes was
really dead. Being informed that he was, it
stated that it had nothing to live for now, and,
lying down solemnly in the roadway, it crumbled
into dust. Then the cart crumbled also, and the
coffin and the tombstone and Parkes all faded
away, and we started to walk back. And the last
thing we remembered was a milestone stating that
it was eighteen million leagues to Sydney, all up-

A Sandhurst (Vic.) bachelor—a women
and children hater also—while travelling to Mel-
bourne the other day, witnessed his fellow-
passenger, a young woman with a baby in her
arms, coolly strip the youngster, sponge it with
water from the train lavatory, powder-puff it all
over, and then put on it a nice, clean pinny.
Another horror is thus added to long railway
journeys.

* # *
One of the Foreign-trade politicians of
N.S. Wales has a curious establishment in which
he lives after the manner of a large and copious
bear in a cave. The floors are washed about once
a year, and the carpets are a mass of rags caked
together by dirt. The grates are always blocked
up with yesterday’s ashes, and they are decorated
with great blots of ancient rust, as if the politician
habitually shot the slops out of his tea-cup at the
fireplace. The remains of the breakfast stand
about on the table till they get mixed up and
consolidated with lunch, and the latter meal
opens out like a telescope and elongates itself till
it hitches on to dinner. The water the politician
washed his hands in yesterday remains in the
basin in the bed-room till he wants to wash
his hands again to-day, and then he shoot
it out of the window, regardless of consequences.
The window-curtains are yellow and mangy, and
hang like a dead man on a gallows ; the window's
are mostly cracked ; the doors are all_ loose and
have a tendency to bang ; and the visitor gener-
ally enters a room head-first, because his feet get
hitched in the door-mat, which is a superannuated
dish-cloth and a man-trap. Things in general are
got rid of in that establishment by jerking them
out into the back-yard, and in the matter of
potato-skins and cobwebs the place stands alone.
And amid all this desolation the politician sits
around on a layer of dust, like an animal roosting
on an ash-heap, and seems quite content. His
children fall down-stairs promiscuously, for there
is always a bucket of water standing by accident ina
dark angle of the staircase, left there apparently by
somebody who was going to mop the house up, but
who abandoned the job in disgust and fled. His
clean shirt is usually in the cupboard on top of
the loaf, and his boots are in the dog-kennel. As
to his wife, she is either on top of the roof or down
in the cellar, for she is about the most casual
female going, and very hard to find when wanted.
A SUGGESTION.
MASHAM: “ I want some nice little thing for my wife— something that’ll please her. Perhaps
you could suggest something.”
SUE: “Yes, I think a telegram saying that a house had fallen on you, ought to please
her.”
Most of the work of the house is done by a little
girl, the daughter of an old, dead friend, whom
the politician took in when her father departed
this life —rumour has it that he shed tears by that
parent’s bedside, and told him that his_ child would
never, never want for a home and a lovin art so
long as he (the politician) was above ground. And
as that little girl has to wash up, and carry
buckets of water, and cook, and fly round on
errands, and make herself generally useful at all
hours of the day and night without salary, it is
evident that her adopted father kept his word.

# » *
We were invited to inspect a work of art
the other day. It was a costly thing, and purely
English, for the owner didn’t go in for Australian
art-lie explained that it lacked soul, and was
utterly deficient in that deep and ramifying com-
pleteness of expression and that profound yet sub-
dued consciousness which breathes in an ethereal
minor key through the masterpieces of the British
school. We suggested that perhaps the Austra-
lians didn’t slam on the paint hard enough, but he
said it wasn’t that—they lacked breadth of con-
sciousness. Then he showed us the picture and
struck a paralytic attitude in front of it. It was
a twilight-scene with a dark-green sea in it, and
dark-green clouds hurrying across a pale-green,
stormy sky. There was a greenish shore in the
foreground, and a blackish-green rock with mois-
ture and green slime on it. Beside it stood a tall,
vallerv-greenery female in corpse-coloured gar-
ments, and sadly -slowly-thoughtfully she
scraped her green face \ ith the nails of her green
hand, and looked out of her green eye at the sea
and the sky ; and everything was green and solemn
and very profound. And the owner looked at it
with profound joy on his face, and said that only
the genuine British artist knew how to put soul in
his work and to insert in it that deep and rever-
ent tenderness of expression of which true art is
composed.

* * *
“ Pentecost ” makes a suggestion :
When a human being is about to be born let him send
word to the State, and let the State appoint a commission
composed of one Republican and one Democratic political
ward boss, one monopolist, one trades-unionist, one
Malthusian, and one anti-Malthusian political economist,
and one representative of each religious sect, and let
them decide whether he may come into being or not.
What is the use of having a State unless we can make it
useful in guarding the exits and entrances to life ?
# # *
There was a fire one night recently in a
wooden house situated in a lonely locality some
miles from Auckland (M.L.), and the place blazed
joyously. By-and-bye the glare attracted the
attention of a man who was passing about a mile
away, and he ran like a racehorse for the scene of
action. As he got nearer he heard the screams of
a wild, distracted female, and, thinking that some
woman was being combusted, he ran still harder.
He took a bee-line across country, burst over
fences, and rushed right through gates because he
hadn’t time to open them. And when he got to
the spot ho found there a burning house already
more than half-consumed, and a female tearing
madly up and down the road, rending her hair
and crying in frantic accents “My child! my
child !” He took in the scene at a glance —the
heart-broken parent, the flaming house, and the
frizzled babe inside—and his manly heart rose
within him, and he took a header through the
window to save the offspring or perish in the
attempt.
Two other men came along after a while
and hauled him out. Most of his hair was gone,
and his only suit of clothes was wrecked, and his
preservers had to quench him by rolling him
violently in the dust. Then he sat up and
asked them to tell the heart-broken mother
gently that he hadn’t been able to find her
infant. They looked round for that shattered
parent, but couldn’t see any traces of her. Next
they started to search for her, and a crowd
gradually collected, and at last they learned that
she had been seen three-quarters of a mile away
walking contentedly towards home. She didn t
live in the burnt house at all, and she was an old
maid with no infant. She had simply been pass,
ing along the road, and had found a deserted
shanty on fire, and had there and then gone
into hysterics, and shouted for a lost child on
general principles. When she was tired yelling
she had recovered, and fixed up her hair, and
gone home, leaving the hero groping among the
ruins for a non-existent baby. These matters
were explained to him .as he sat by the roadside
and shook the ashes out of his hair, and he and
all the crowd took an oath that they would burst
into no more flaming buildings to look for any-
body’s offspring, unless the mother produced her
marriage certificate and the register of the infant s
birth in the first place, and supported her asser-
tions with an affidavit.
# * *
“Rolf Boldrewood’s ” new novel, “A
Sydney-Side Saxon,” is as readable and almost as
vigorous as his earlier works. It retains also
those anachronisms and those occasional incoher-
ences of stvle which are the characteristics of this
notable Australian writer. Four books in two
years, the whole of them published 10,000 miles
distant from the author’s home, and consequently
from the opportunity of revision, will account for
a good deal, but surely now that Mr. Browne has
established his reputation it is about time that he
should use the file upon his productions. W hen the
crudity disappears, the English critic cannot pos-
sibly find any other point to discuss, respecting
which his judgment would be worth a brass
farthing, and then he may give us a rest.
The Victorian Government has decided
that in future free railway passes shall be given
only to aboriginals, destitute persons, or charit-
able institutions. Therefore, the distinguished
globe-trotter will have to travel as a “destitute
person” or a “charitable institution,” or else
paint himself black and go partially naked.
In a northern railway-station not long
since a real live Judge was waiting for a train. A
crowd of school children were also present.
Having been provided with a chair, the public
functionary sat down and proceeded to pose in a
majestic attitude. Then suddenly he rose up and
spoke: “Station-master, come heah instantly.”
That official arrived at a run. “Lower those
blinds instantly ; there are some beastly boys out
there who had the impudence to lawf at me—at
me, sah, me, a Judge of the colony, sah! ”

* * *
There were several hitches in the proceed-
ings at Hobart when sports were held as an item
of welcome to the Auxiliary Squadron. One of
the worst was a cat’s-chorus competition between
the City Band and the Garrison Band. Both
bands had been lying low, and when the moment
arrived for Gov. Hamilton and Ad. Scott to para-
lyse the gathering, the old question of precedence
got up. So both bands tackled the Foreign
Anthem in separate keys, and the result sounded
like an earthquake in a pan-emporium, accom-
panied by dogs, yells, and fits.
* # *
The Victorian Sketching Club is a collec-
tion of about 22 enthusiasts, who have a rough
time of it in their pursuit of Art. They start
every Saturday at noon and travel into the wilder-
ness, and sit upon damp grass, and sketch cows,
and old boilers, and gasworks, and other features
of scenery. Last week the party arranged to visit
Black Rock, but the weather was bad and only
one sodden devotee turned up. He went to the
Rock alone and had just made his first pot-hook
when the blast arose and blew his sketch right out
of the world, and drove his coat-tail into his ear
and his ear into his pocket. He cursed a little
after that, and then the wind fell, and he got some
more paper out and was just about to knock spots
off Murillo when another hurricane buried his hat
and his paint and other materials under three feet
of mud. Then a bullock rushed him, and some-
body wanted to sue him for trespass, and he
started for home.
# * *
Lady Hamilton gave one of her dreadful
“ at homes ” in honour of the visit of the Auxiliary
Squadron to Hobart. These awful entertainments
are a superior class of tea-meeting in evening
dress. There is no recognised dancing, though
one or two waltzes are allowed after what is
facetiously called “ supper ”—a ghastly repast of
sugared buns and cruelly-weak negus. A high
intellectual tone is supposed to pervade these
entertainments; everybody looks intensely un-
comfortable, and nobody knows what on earth
to do, but, then, they are very “toney,” and the
proprieties are observed with agonising severity.
HIS PAINFUL HISTORY.
His heart was set on getting cash; lie late and
early sought it,
And cared not what the nature of the venture was
that brought it.
A miner at the start, ho delved among the
Northern ridges,
And afterwards he contracts took for mending
roads and bridges.
He next discovered that the town gave room for
more expansion,
And thither came and furnished out a fine subur-
ban mansion.
A bank one day he set afloat, a silver-mine the
morrow,
And bank and mine to all concerned brought joy
instead of sorrow.
He formed a syndicate and built a fleet of coastal
sailors,
And sent a company away to toil as South Sea
whalers.
He bought ramshackle cabins near the heart of
population
And turned them into dwellings for the people of
. high station.
And gold flowed out of everything on which he
operated,
Until he was the proudest man that ever specu-
lated.
’Twas then he took it in his head to start a great
diurnal,
A print that would show out ahead of every other
journal.
He nourished it upon his gold and afterwards he
fed it
With mortgages and lOU’s and every kind 6f
credit.
But people named the print “ a rag ” and called its
contents silly,
And now he’s back again among the ridges with
his billy !

A few weeks ago, Providence, or the other
fellow, got level with a N.S.W. Judge in a really
commendable way. He was on circuit in the
back-blocks, and was working at high pressure
to' get back to Sydney in time for a pompous
gubernatorial feed. The last day of the circuit he
kept the court going for 15 hours at a stretch,
and it was past midnight when the business was
disposed of. There were four young children
.among the witnesses to be examined ; it was a
cold day and raining incessantly, and for six
mortal hours those damp infants were kept hang-
ing about the steps of the courthouse, weary,
miserable, dejected, and full of coughs, snuffles,
and influenza. Then in the small hours of the
morning the prisoner was acquitted, or sent to gaol,
or sentenced to be hanged for 15 years with hard
labour and two floggings, or something, and the
next day the Judge started back by coach. On the
road Providence—or the other fellow—got square
with him, as already mentioned. The roads
were fearful, and the night came on black, murky,
and utterly impenetrable. The coach got bogged
11 times, and the judge got out and hauled and
shoved like a demon in a hundred sloughs and
mud-holes. Then a horse fell, and the Judge
helped to get it up again, and the animal kicked
a quart of mud into his eye. Next the harness
burst, and it was necessary to walk a mile and a
half and steal a clothes-line to repair it with.
Last of all, in the deepest bog of the lot, a wheel
gave out. At this stage the Judge said he would
take thh only horse of the team that wasn’t
utterly collapsed, and go on by himself, but the
driver told him briefly that he would see him
further first, and climbing on the animal himself
he cantered off into the slush and darkness.
Then the man of law gritted his teeth
together, and started to walk. It was 16 miles
to the railway ; the slush was two feet deep; the
rain came down _with a dull, monotonous persis-
tence, and the wind was moaning like a weeping
ghost among the hills. Two miles from the town
the driver—coming back with afresh vehicle—met
a spectre that was caked all over with mud and
using awful language on the highway, and conveyed
it into town in the gloaming of the early morning.
The gubernatorial feed was over—it had taken
place while the Judge was ploughing through the
dank morasses and dragging his legs out of in-
numerable bogs and ditches. There had been
champagne and burgundy, and rich choice soups,
and boned turkey, and luscious fruits, and much
grovel; and he had been thinking of them while
he crawled along with his clothes sodden, and his
boots in a state of pulp, and his bat soaked into
ruins, and his collar feeling like a damp halter,
and pints of slush in every pocket. And when,
about 4 a.m., he was thrown out on the doorstep
of a country public-house, he probably felt that
things were about level at last.
* * #
Governor Hopetouu attended the private
inspection of Melbourne Zoo. and said a few
things, some of which created a favourable im-
pression, while others didn’t. First he remarked
that the Zoo. was a credit to the “province.”
This shows that he has been reading The Bulle-
tin, as that paper abolished the term “colony ” and
put “province” in its stead ; but it excited the
politicians present, for, according to the Victorian
standard, “ colony ” is the only genuine word, as
may be seen by the wrapper on the bottle, all
others being false and seditious. Secondly, Hopc-
toun tried to whistle a Scotch tune, and his voice
has an awful tendency to fly off the handle, in a
manner of speaking. 'Thirdly, he tried to feel the
macaw’s teeth, and that amiable bird swallowed
the viceroy’s arm right up to the elbow and had
to be beaten off with a sapling, else it would have
swallowed the viceroy altogether. Taking it all
round, Hopetoun had a good time.

Some unknown person who doesn’t sign
his letter writes as follows from London :
Mr. J. Troubridge Critchell presents his compliments to
the Editor of the Sydney Bulletin, and begs to forward
for his inspection a letter which has first appeared in the
Times written by Mr. Critchell. As a communication
addressed to the English press last May, giving the views
of the Rev. Charles Clark on the Australian Labour Ques-
tion, was submitted by the editor to the delicate and
refined criticism for which Tub Bulletin has become cele-
brated, Mr. Critchell thinks it possible that the present
innocent effort of an old Australian to benefit his colony
may merit the censure of the editor. Acting on his belief
that the abuse of the Sydney Bulletin is praise indeed,
Mr. Critchell hastens to afford to the editor the oppor-
tunity to express once again his dislike to all colonists
who venture to hope that Australasia has nobler aims
than those preached in the columns of the Svdnev
Bulletin. j
We have inspected the letter which “first appeared
in the Times,” and find it to be a wild woosh of
figures about the number of cows in Queensland.
It presents the general appearance of a multiplica-
tion table struck by lightning, but otherwise it is
quite harmless. Therefore, we will let it pass,
and Mr. Critchell can exist a while longer. At
the same time that person is hereby admonished
to walk a chalk-line in future, else The Bulletin
may rise up and abolish him.
* * *
The Salvation frenzy is spreading. The
other day a bleary old gin was to be seen wander-
ing down Queen-street, Brisbane, in full Salva-
tion costume, with her ancient, weather-worn nose
shaded by her poke bonnet, and her capacious feet
flopping along in the narrow way that leads unto
the bright and shining gate. She was saved, and
had sworn off, as could be seen by the awful look
of thirst in her haggard eye, and the hungry way
she stopped and sniffed at the door of each bar
she came to. Also there was a sort of uneasy hop
in her walk, which had evidently been acquired
by dancing through the mud in pursuit of the
tambourine. She was a dejected old black wreck,
)ufc she was still recognisable. Years ago she
belonged to a fragment of a tribe that camped on
the river-bank near Hamilton, and in these days
she was a fighting female, and it was a refreshing
spectacle to see her knock her husband out on the
highway. Then when the old man had enough of
it she would swear copiously, and dash road-metal
through the hotel windows, and curse through the
broken panes, and when the barman came out to
remonstrate she would leap high in the air with a
yeii, and kick his ear with the soles of her feet.
And now she plods humbly tl'rough theslush behind
the drum, with her poke bonnet put on hind side
in iront, and her military days are over. What
a fail is there for the daughter of the soil.

A c 6i'tain bloated landlord of Fitzroy
{Victoria) is hereby invited to come outside and
shy mud and ignominy at his own degraded horse-
carcase. A young married woman, deserted by
hei husband, had rented a room in his house for
some months. When her confinement arrived she
was almost starving. She got over her trouble
somehow (Providence only knows how), but before
the child was two weeks old the mother and infant
were both reduced to utter destitution. Then the
woman, who had been honest so long as she had
n0 , 01 !® to s h ar e her misery, stole the blankets
and pillows from the bed and pawned them for
food and medicine, and her landlord promptly ran
her in, 1 here are seven different Parliaments in
Australasia, and they all peg in making laws for
about nine months in the year, but among them
all there isn’t even a single law, so far as we know
to authorise tying a preacher to that landlord and
shoving him head first into an active volcano.
Put if a large, solid, whole-souled curse can pene-
trate his moral hide he has it unanimously.

the gilded denizens of “ onr village ” has set up an
Bft. Gin. snake, and when he wanders about on
the shore with the reptile hanging out of his
pocket he is a pleasing sight. Sometimes he has
it suspended round his neck for a change, and at
other times he carries it in his hand, and then for
variety he will throw it over his shoulder and let
the end of it trail behind him, and the rest of the
time he carries it anyhow. When he sits down any-
where the snake coils up beside him, and his dog
hangs round and licks it; or sometimes the snake
licks the dog for a change and makes that animal
somewhat damp and slimy. At night, we pre-
sume, the reptile hangs itself up on its owner’s
bed-post, or else throws itself loosely over the
towel-rail, but this is mere conjecture.
# * *
The English commercial papers report
that Indian tea is rapidly superseding the Chinese
article in the markets of Europe. We are glad to
hear this. There was something that looked like
a fragment of Joss floating in our cup the other
morning; it wasn’t very distinguishable, but it
certainly seemed to be a bit of a Chinese god of
some description ; and we object to drinking gods
out of an Asiatic pantheon. When the Chow
begins to drop his deities into the tea, he is
treading on the finer feelings of the white race,
and something is going to happen.
* * #
According to an English paper there is a
girl in one of the London workhouses who speaks
a language that nobody can understand except
herself. It is presumed that she does understand it,
but even this point is not certain. She looks like
an English female, but the parish has lost the run
of her, in a manner of speaking, and nobody is
quite certain where she came from. All the pro-
fessors within reach have tried to locate her, but
without success. Some of them think she invented
the language herself, and others imagine that she
is the last girl of some lost nation which every-
body has forgotten, but nobody gets any nearer a
solution of the mystery.
* * *
According to one of a series of sketches
now running in Paris Figaro, Napoleon the
Small, notoriously catholic in his love for the sex,
was crossing one of the salons in the Tuilleries ere
the lamps were lit, and, attracted by a shapely
ankle that protruded from a mass of drapery on a
sofa, pinched the limb appended. The head that
emerged thereupon was that of the Bishop of
Nancy.
* # *
Mudgee (N.S.W.) Hospital Committee
passed an absurd resolution in ’BB that the regular
hospital doctor must nob call in a second medical
man without previously getting the consent of the
visiting committee. While he is hunting up
the committee the patient must, of course, post-
pone dying ! The doctor broke this rule recently,
and now the committee want him to pay the
second professional’s fee, amounting to twelve
guineas.
* # *
Fifteen thousand pounds worth of old
man was lying on his death-bed, and his wife was
in an interesting condition. He called for a
lawyer and dictated a will destined, as he thought,
to meet any emergency. If the child was a boy,
the mother was to get £5OOO for herself, while
£lO,OOO was to be invested on trust for the son
and heir. If the new-comer was a girl, the
mother was to get £lO,OOO, while only £5OOO went
to a trust fund. Then the old man died happy.
The widow shortly afterwards gave birth to twins
—a boy and a girl. And now there is much riot
among the lawyers as to whether the boy-half of
the twins is not entitled to £lO,OOO and the girl-
half to £5OOO, and the mother to —the balance, it
being argued that her legacy was purely a con-
tingent one. Ihe Court informs the widow that
she has placed herself outside the will by an act
of contributory negligence, but the widow wants
to know what the Court thinks she should have
done about it, and the Court can’t tell her. Just
where the exact division comes in isn’t at all clear,
but by the time the lawyers have fixed it up,
there’ll be nothing to divide.

A prominent London Theosophist claims
to have received from the deceased Madame
Blavatsky a most depressing occult communication
from which it appears that H.P.B. has been
unfortunate in the “hence.” “ She has lost her
clothes and is now wandering about the meads of
Asphodel in a state of nudity,” is the gist of her
troubles.
