* # *
The Tasmanian customs authorities have—
or one of them has—prohibited the introduction
of “ Robert Elsmere ” as a book that is unfit for
circulation. The Bulletin doesn’t absolutely
love the book in question—the hero who is always
inspecting his own moral “innards” with'a
microscope grows wearisome—but the official ass
who would pile up his anathema on it as an offen-
sive publication hasn’t intelligence enough to get
out of bed without legal assistance.
* # *
A London clergyman who didn’t desire to
insert either himself or his family in the census,
owing to some details concerning his marriage
which badly wanted to be suppressed, dodged the
collector by driving about London in cabs with
ms whole family all that night and most of the
following day. They couldn’t stop anywhere for
fear of being included in a census paper, and the
night was very cold, and there were eleven of them
all told.
* # #
An Anti-Brokers’ Brigade has been started
in London. When the bailiffs take possession of
a member’s goods, the brigade comes to the rescue,
pays up the back rent, and generally assaults the
officer as well.
* * *
Old, sunburnt Bananalanders know how
to observe their democratic points. When Gover-
nor and Lady Norman visited a seaside resort of
that province recently, a veteran drove his Ex.
round in one vehicle, while a bright young damsel
took the Governess round in a po’shay. “That’s
a pretty place,” remarked General Norman,
pointing to a picturesque residence. “ Who lives
there?” “ The mother of the lady who is driving
your missus, boss.”
* * *
“ A Housekeeper ” writes to a London
editor, growlingly, that, if he wants to persuade
people to be teetotallers, he ought also to suggest
some way of making either provisions cheaper or
incomes larger. Says the hash-mistress :
I can feed a man on ten shillings a week, with plain
wholesome food and a quart of beer a day, but a teeto-
taller eats such an immense quantity that he cannot be
fed at the same price. They say “ A Christian has the
privilege of self-denial,” and denies himself the pleasure
of drinking. Yea, verily ; but you should see him eat!
* * #
The people of Cairns (Q.) are troubled
because they got up an expensive printed and
illuminated grovel to the captain of the ironclad
Ringarooma, and he left the document lying in a
corner of the hotel when he went away. At latest
a teetotal lecturer had got the publication and
was showing it off as a picture of the inflamed
stomach of a drunkard.
The S.M. Herald declares that it can
possess what it calls its soul in patience anent the
intentions of the Dibbs Ministry on “ the subjects
of electoral reform, courts of conciliation, and
other similar ones.” Then it adds with the grate-
fulness of a corn-fed stomach :
We may be sure that when anxiety is expressed as to
what the Government policy will be no reference is in-
tended to second-rate matters such as these.
What, then, is the S.M. Herald’s idea of a
first-rate” matter? The “Bone-Idler,” we pre-
sume, and the Echo’s solitary joke, and the con-
gregational tea-meeting, and the ponderous review
of the ponderous scientific book upon the hind legs
of microbes, and things like these. And it will
worry drearily over these leaden topics till some
day it falls over a second-rate matter and breaks
its back and dies in some hospital for decrepit and
incurable literature.
It is most convenient to have a lirst-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
According to the D.T report, Johnston,
M.L.A., declares that if Dibbs tries “to tinker
with the tariff, ’ the Labour party will say “ out
you go.” The Bulletin hereby tells Johnston
that he is putting both feet in it. The country
elected most of the Labour members as protec-
tionists. By doing so it demonstrated that the
two things it wanted were Protection and Demo-
cratic Reform, and if it is compelled by the foolish-
ness of its own representatives to do without
one of them, it may rise and throw out
Johnston and get some more efficient democrat in
his stead. Moreover, the party claims to have
sunk the fiscal issue,” yet it voted for Foreign-
trade in return for Parkes’s promised concessions.
Therefore, if Dibbs will offer similar concessions it
must now vote for Protection in return, otherwise
it hasn’t sunk the fiscal issue at all, but is going
bald-headed for cheap Chinese goods and imported
British shoddy and the extinction of local indus-
try in general.
Now that Carruthers has vamoosed Into
the vasty oblivion which sooner or later swallows
up every hanger-on to Parkes’ coat-tails, what is
going to become of that wretched School History ’
The first thing the new Minister for Education
should do is to knock the whole thing on the head,
although the procedure would have pretty much
the character of sticking a knife into the dead.
We are not referring to Lusk’s association with
the book, -but to the twaddleful, petticoated
creatures of the Department, when we say that
the thing has been tinkered at till it is a mere
hash of Barrington’s, Braim’s, Lang’s and Suther-
land’s histories with a strong dose of Freetrade
essence extracted from Parkes’s speeches and
Carruthers’ Young Men’s Society essays. If the
thing wanted incessant patching" up, why was the
prize-money paid over, and if it wanted nothing
of the kind, why was it tinkered, and why has it
not been published ?

Safe democratic motto ; “ Where you see
a crowned head, hit it.”
The Queensland Patriotic League is a
body built on the same lines as the N.S. W. bogus
“ National ’’ Association. It has also exactly the
same programme, one of its objects being “to
uphold a just balance of electoral representation.”
It is now upholding the said balance. The
members of the League are all bulgy money-
lenders, bankers, merchants, squatters, and the
like, and almost all the J.sP. belong to it. These
individuals are now revising the electoral roll, and
are knocking off the names of working men by
hundreds. The process is very simple. The
revising justices call no evidence, ask no questions,
give no reasons, and offer no chance of explana-
tion. They simply say that they have “cause to
believe ” that about two or ten consecutive pages
of names are wrongfully on the roll because the
owners have “changed their residence,” or some-
thing of the sort, and what is the “cause” that
led them to believe that circumstance isn’t ex-
plained. Then they knock off six or eight streets
of working men in one act, and the job is
finished.
A new penny weekly has been started in
Melbourne for the benefit of those quaint
fogies who are fond of bartering old socks for
Limburger cheeses and bustles for cheap
oleographs. It is called the Exchange and
Mart, and some of its advertisements read
humorously:—One joker wants “a fender and
hearth-rug ” for “ an odd lot of carpenter’s tools
a matron asks for “ a portable copper and peram-
bulator ” for a sewing-machine; an artist offers a
“tin box of oil paints” for a “violin bow;”
another musical aspirant would give a carpet for
a “ second-hand violin a chap who must have
lost his vocation demands a “cedar sideboard and
four chairs ” for an “ incubator ” with “ 200 eggs,
good order ’’—which means probably half-hatched
or half-addled ; a sport offers a “ young talking
magpie” for a “Newfoundland pup a come-
downer is willing to give a “bowl of goldfish in
exchange for six antimaccars ” (sic); and —grisly
terror of all!—an afflicted cuss offers a “second-
hand set of false teeth and plait of golden hair ”
for “a cork leg!” There are lots of less
interesting offers of barter. The writer will be
glad to exchange an impediment in his speech and
a Revised Version for a squint and a good, whole-
some, reliable, whisky breath.
Parkes’ last official act was to find profit"
able jobs at the public expense for about eleven of
his broken - down friends, hangers - on, and
acquaintances. Some of the gang were unknown
to the public, and why they should have billets is
incomprehensible; the rest are too w r ell-known,
and why they shouldn’t have billets is obvious to
the naked eye. The jobs have mostly good
salaries attached, and no visible hard work.
About six nondescript cripples—one of them over
seventy years of age—are to do some unspecified
gyration in connection with the Chicago Exhibi-
tion. Somebody else is to fool round in connec-
tion with an alleged Exhibition in Tasmania—
which Exhibition Parkes formerly denounced as
being too small a show to be worth official notice.
The eternal Oscar Meyer—who seems to live to a
great extent on exhibitions —is to look after some-
thing. The still more eternal John Davies—the
public apparition with the three old hats—is to be
some kind of an official Bumble about the
National Park. All of them are to send in the
bill to their bleeding country, and all these
appointments were made by Parkes after he had
resigned office, and when he had ceased to have
any moral right to appoint anybody. Whether
he had or had not any legal right is an open
question, and one that is worth a good deal of
inspection.
An 18-ffc. alligator got impounded in a
Chinaman’s fish-trap up at Cairns, N.Q., the other
day, and most of the local population turned
out to try and effect its death or capture. Vain
attempts were made by daring hunters to spear
the saurian, to waddy it into insensibility, or to
boathook it hors de combat. Then an enterprising
torpedo enthusiast laid a mine of dynamite under
its off-hind wing, and started the fuse going, while
everybody retired to a safe distance. Nothing
came of it, however, and, after a half-hour’s
anxious waiting, the assemblage returned to
reconnoitre and found that the reptile had
swallowed dynamite, fuse and all. A combined
attack was now made to capture the scaly one
alive, according to the rules of “ Baste the Bear,”
the’gator being “bear.” The ironclad varmint
proved excellent in this rdle and after chasing the
bipeds all over the trap for a diverting hour or so,
during which he gorged four pairs of pants, nine
pairs of shoes and an odd Wellington boot, a
gaiter and a half, three spurs, 17 stockwhips, two
dogs, square yards of wire netting, half a
Mongolian leg, and a few pounds weight of
various explosives, he suddenly steeplechased over
all barriers and took a header into the deep.
These old ways of killing the alligator are no good.
The only sure thing is to blast the animal with
electricity or else induce him to take shares in a
building society.

of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla.

SPORTING NOTIONS
With Carbine, Melos, Vengeance, Dreadnought,
Bungebah, and such olddehisive identities as Tradition and
U hakawai already.scratched for the Melbourne Cup, the
public are watching the belting-market with a dull, lishy
eye, and vaguely wondering what to morrow may bring
forth. Their faith in legs is shaken. Their best idols lie
Baaltered ip (he dust. A good d ea l of their annual en-
tluisiiin is, for the moment, gone bung.
* # #
It is a pity that Carbine’s ligament couldn’t
ha\ e hung together for another two or three weeks,
because the old hero would, in the opinion of hi? trainer
and jockey, have shown the fleld his tail and pulled the
punters through once again. Anyway, he would have
spared his early supporters the grief and pain of trying to
recover the money that has gone down the sink.
* * #
Before Tirailleur came over, the Maoriland
people sent word that he was another Carbine, only more
bo. At Flemington he doesn’t seem to please anybody
though ; he doesn’t look as first-class as his performances,
and the . proverbial screw is probably loose in this good
horse. So with his relative, Cuirassier, apparently, and
with several others too mediocre to he worth mentioning.
Among horses that are being backed .for more or less
money, at time of writing, it is easy to name .several that
no prophet could show reasonable cause for .selecting as
winners. Pigeontoe, Forty Winks, Forest King, Whim-
hrel, Glenlpth, Oxide, G’Naroo, Sir William, The Tyler,
and Stromboli are none of them good enough, on paper,
ti win this Melbourne Cup. Either they have given no
sort of evidence of ability to win a fast-run race over two
miles, or else they have never shown the form necessary
to give a fair “paper” chance of victory under their
weights. Granted, that there may be no real first-
class horses in this year’s race—what has Whimbrel done
to mike him a very likely horse to get home with only
a pound less than Bravo carried? Or do Sir William’s
recent performances suggest that he can repeat the
B avo record? G’Naroo hasn’t a fifty to one chance of
winning under Bst. 1 libs., nor do any of those that
(unshed behind him in the Caulfield Cup catch the eye as
possible good things for the big handicap.
# * *
This is a year for a three-year-old to repeat the
Martiui-llenry trick, provided that a youngster within
coo-ee of Mr. White’s brilliant animal is included
amongst the entries. At present the stable companions,
Zahnski and Strathmore, look like the best of their year,
and if either or both of them start next Tuesday they will
certainly beat two-thirds of the Held. Zalinskicommands
respect as the half-brother to Trenton, but the weight;
to he carried by this fine colt and good recent per-
former is pounds more than any three-year-old has yet
put on the record. Strathmore, on the other hand, is
nicely weighted. By Nordenfeldt out of Correze’s dam.
he is handicapped at five pounds less than Correze carried
into third place last year. A Derby victory, of course
would mean a heavy penalty for Strathmore, but common
sense points to the probability of Zalinski being the stable
horse for the Derby, inasmuch as a win in Saturday’s
event would not increase the latter’s weight for the Clip
Should Strathmore go for the Derby the public may take
it for granted that Zalinski is a stone better colt, or else
that Mr Wilson despairs of getting a 6st. 121bs. boy to
ride Correze’s brother in the two mile race, and is there-
fore indifferent about incurring the penalty which
attaches to the classic blue riband. But it must not be
forgotten that only a phenomenal three-year-old can pull
off the Melbourne Cup under any weight, and nothin"-
short of a four lengths win in the Derby would justify
punters in pinning their entire faith to a youngster for
the £lO,OOO handicap.
*# # ,
To the writer’s way of thinking, Highborn ought
to beat all the horses that finished behind him last
Kovembev save Correze. Muriel is a mare likely to run
well, yet on the Sydney Cup form she has decidedly the
worst of it with Highborn. However, when one comes to
think of it, very few of last year’s competitors will turn
up again this journey. Between Highborn and Grevgown
it is difficult to choose ; probably most students of the
weight-for-age scale will make .out the mysterious grey
horse to be “ better in ” than the trusty Highborn. Cer-
tainly his performances indicate that he has a more
brilliant dash than the older horse, and it is a fact that
the astute Maitland swears Grevgown has a soft thin" on
at Flemington. The Bulletin will take Correze to beat
Greygown, nevertheless. Looking through the book it
appears that Correze has twice won from him, givino-
him nine pounds on one occasion. On the score of looks’
Correze has the advantage, too. He is a sweet
little horse, another Mentor in make and shape
and about his ganreness there can be no question at all.’
VVith good health and a good run Correze will not finish
behmd more than one or two horses at most, and he
should be the best “place” spec, in the race. Of which
there is no manner of doubt, no possible doubt whatever.
Megaphone, although bound to be up amongst them at
the finish, cannot be regarded as a dead bird. He ran
like a moderate animal behind Marvel at Caulfield while
to win with 9st. odd in the saddle he would need to be a
clinker, anyway. Cobham’s form is very difficult to
arrive at, and his second to Marvel, at Randwick, is an
unknown quantity. Marvel might perchance have been
equal to giving him another two stone. At Flemington
however, Cobham is well liked, his breeding is unexcep-
tionable, and it would be a rash tipster who said this fine
horse had no chance for the Cup. Au contraire, as the
Chinamen say, there are no substantial grounds for nut-
ting one’s boots on him. *
* * *
At time of writing Malvolio and Paris are
equal first favourites. Malvolio has one noteworthy per-
formance to his credit, viz., a defeat of Highborn in a
email race and when in receipt of ten pounds. The son
of Malua is a handsome horse, and is alleged to be
capable of staying two miles, but there is absolutely
no reason for supposing that he is better qualified
to win a Melbourne Cup than the overrated Benzon.
trained in the same stable, has been for the past two
years. On paper, Malvolio is not good enough to back at
a short price, let him win the Cup or not. Paris strikes
one as a quadruped of quite another colour. His last
three tries have been wins, and his Doncaster Handicap
race stamped him as a flyer. In the Rous Handicap,
again, he beat Correze easily at seven pounds, the weight
he receives from the chestnut in the Cup. Finally Paris
comes from the dangerous Marvel crowd, has been tried
equal to winning a Melbourne Cup, and goes like a little
steam-engine on the track. All going well, he will start a
warm first favourite, and The Bulletin expects him to
win. Back Paris and die. Should he break down before
ine race, you can retire into a corner and meditate over
the glorious uncertainty of the turf.

Jem Burge is ill, and his meeting with Billy
Murphy has been postponed for a month. Burge has had
some terrible smashings about the body, and, iron man as
ne is, the writer has an idea that the punishment is now
beginning to tell upon him, and that the best has been
seen of his fighting. With Burge out of the way, Griffo
will no doubt again bob up serenely.

Round the Shops and Shows.
Scarcely a week passes but some new brand
of soap is advertised which claims to surpass all
others in point of cleansing properties, &c. No
u°uht they are all very good in their way, but
Mile’s Cold Water Soap” is the only soap
which, not only is a labour-saver, but is also a
luel-'saver. No fire at all is necessary, and no
rubbing; and as it is sold at a mere fraction over
the cost of common, soap, housekeepers who
neglect to give it atrial deserve to undergo all the
usual discomforts of the ever-dreaded washing-
day. Once tested —always used. It can be
obtained from any grocer.

Mr. Leon Harris, of Market - street
(between Hunter’s Boot Palace and Evening
JNews office), states that he gives the very highest
price for rough pearls and opals. He also wishes
it to be known that he modernises old-fashioned
jewellery, mends watches, brooches, etc., and as
a gem-setter he is not excelled in Sydney. Any-
thing pertaining to a lapidary’s art could not be
placed in better hands than his. The address is
42 Market-street, next to Hunter’s Boot Palace
* * #
One of the leading questions of the day is
—“What will destroy silver-fish and moths?”
ihe idea that camphor or pepper will suffice to
keep these small fiends at bay is exploded long
since, as thousands of housekeepers know to their
sorrow. Ladies will, therefore, rejoice to know
that one Matusa Komo, of Tokio, Japan, has
discovered a certain destroyer of these pests, and
has named it “No-mi-yc-kee.” It has been proved
to be most efficacious in destroying insect-life, and
should be of great value to every householder.
The sole agents are Messrs. Jamieson and 00.,
of Bond and Hamilton streets, Sydney.

Nineteen out of every twenty of those
unfortunates who are suffering a recovery from
that popular malady, influenza, find that every
now and then they are attacked by such fits of
utter prostration, consequent upon the suffering
they have undergone, that life is but a burden to
them. 1 his shows they need a strong brain and
nerve tonic, and no better can be recommended
than Halliday’s Celery, Beef, and Iron Tonic.
Celery as a nerve tonic is simply marvellous,
whilst the extract of beef is well-known as a
strength-giver, and iron as an enricher and purifier
of the blood has no rival. By the scientific com-
bination of these three grand properties the'very
best and most palatable Tonic of the day is
arrived at. The agents for this are Jamieson and
Co., Bond and Hamilton-streets.

What encouragement for a young fellow
to sweep the board at the University when the
paper gives portraits of footballers every week?
Deputationist to Premier Munro, last
week: “Will you give us this next session?”
Munro : “Goodness gracious, man, where will we
all be next session ? ”
Athletes should read this. Laurence
Foley, Esq., the retired champion, speaks : “ I
must candidly admit that I have used Marshall’s
Tonic Nervine for years and always found it
wonderfully effective when feeling a little off, and
I not only speak from my own experience, but
have heard numbers of the boys (athletes) say
that for training purposes they have found nothing
equal to it when taken in a little water twice a
day.”
The infant who plays at hanging is one of
the pleasant results of the modern social system.
The other day sundry children at fronton (Ohio,
U.S.) organised a court, and three youngsters —-
two brothers and a sister —were solemnly tried
and sentenced to death. Then they were locked
up in a tool-chest —presumed to be the condemned
cell—and the others forgot to hang them —in fact
they forgot all about them and went home. Result
arrived at a day or so later—three dead infants in
a chest.
I Sixteen years ago, near Clermont, a
| Queensland police officer whipped one of his
black troopers to death with a split-leather saddle-
girth. The mates of the dead boy reported the
matter, and the officer was arrested on the charge
of murder, and committed—bail, of course, being
refused. However, the trial hung fire from term
to term, and in the meantime the prisoner’s wife
came into a fortune. The oracle was worked in
some way, and the officer was released on bail—
£soo in his own recognisances, and £5OO from a
friend. Of course, the bail was jumped, and from
that day to this Queensland has known him no
more. The legend now runs that he is still alive
and prosperous, and can be found any day, if the
Griffith Government wants him. Possibly, how-
ever, the Griffith Government doesn’t want him.
THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST. JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
lit. It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely
a relief, and in no sense a cure-all; it is the pro-
duct of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it soothes and sub-
dues, heals and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer
surface, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently ; search-
mgly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
• >th. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
lor rheumatism, neuralgia, sprains, bruises,
stiff joints, swellings, and similar ailments St.
■lacobs Oil has positively no equal if used accord-
t° the directions which accompany each bottle.
Aches and pains succumb in a truly marvellous
manner to the potent influence of this world-famed
remedy. thousands of testimonials have from
tune to time been published in the Australian
press—- testimonials from all ranks and conditions
of men—from salesmen, judges, eminent divines,
barristers, the medical profession, merchants,
athletes, &c. ; and although a large standing
reward has been offered to any one who could
produce proof that any one of these was not
strictly genuine, none have dared to question their
authenticity. Under these circumstances is it to
be wondered at that the sale of St. Jacobs Oil
greatly exceeds the combined sales of all other
advertised lotions, liniments, embrocations, &c.,
in the world ? No home can afford to be without
a bottle of it always handy, and purchasers would
do well to observe that they get the genuine
article, and not one of the many spurious prepara-
tions made up to trade upon the enormous reputa-
tion which St. Jacobs Oil has built up for itself
upon the solid foundation of merit.

THE ENTERPRISING YANKEE.
I lo produce a watch so simple in construction'
I that it could be sold cheaper than any other, and
I still be a reliable timekeeper, has been the problem
for watchmakers the world over. The Swiss
| developed the making of watches by hand to the
I highest point, at the same time cheapening hand
labor to the utmost possible extent. The
I Americans sought a solution in a different
direction by making the parts of their watches by
machinery, and interchange. However, the cost
of manufacture still remained too great for the
watch of the million. An enterprising Yankee
company finally solved the problem by dispensing
with the greater number of parts of a watch
(reducing the number from 160 to 57!), making all
of them by machinery, and so easy of adjustment
that expert labor was not needed to put them to-
[ gether. This crowning achievement in the history
of watchmaking was accomplished by the Water-
bary Watch Company in their Waterbury Watch
which, though cheap, is as perfect in its parts and
as reliable in its time-keeping as any watch made.

CRAIG & AITKEN, 680 George-st., Sydney.
The Melbourne beaks the other day fined
one woman twenty shillings for being drunk, and
another one ten shillings for a murderous assault.
Which goes to show that the average beak should
be assaulted himself to give him a more general
idea of things.
We have received—presumably for review
—a wierd-looking publication, on the cover of
which is a picture of a lady with her head in the
fire. Another lady is holding her down, and in
retaliation she is taking a huge mouthful out of
the other lady and tearing her hair. There are
sundry broken chairs and shattered gin-bottles
about the room, and a gaunt black cat is cater-
wauling on the table. The contents of the volume
are well up, in a general way, to the standard of
the frontispiece, and are suggestive of a moral
ghoul stirring up an ancient slop-barrel with a
dead snake.
Charters Towers (according to the local
Evening News) is a suicidal maze of unprotected
shafts, unfenced lakes, ragged masses of broken
machinery, heaps of rubbish, tailings, holes, bogs,
pitfalls, ravines, ruts, gutter-ways, stinks, Chinese,
goats, dogs, aldermen and other frightful var-
mints. Says the paper: “ There never was a
town on the face of the globe where suicide is less
necessary, and where it is easier to make a decent
thing out of life-insurance, whether as an agent or
policy-holder.” Good—
Time was, at Broken Hill, when a rever-
ential worshipper of a putty-footed idol presented
the then Municipal Council with a portrait of
Parkes. At that time, hanging space for works
of art was not available, so the mayor lugged
the portrait home and stuck it in his dining-room.
The Broken Hill town hall was built in due
course, and when the old mayor resigned his seat
he also handed over the portrait to be hung in the
official premises. Strangely enough, the Council
cursed when they -saw it, and seemed to take the
thing as a personal injury. In fact, the burninsr
question now in Broken Hill is : “ Where shall
we hang this ’ere Parkes?” The new mayor
won’t have him in his parlour ; the aldermen look
coldly on the painted apparition; the librarian
says his premises are crowded up; the fire brigade
regret their space is limited ; and the town-clerk
has taken the portrait in temporarily and under
protest, and wants it shifted at once. Some day
Parke’s portrait will be dashed down an aban-
doned shaft, to judge from present appearances.
Two negroes, breaking gaol at Raleigh,
U.S.A., weVe awarded 39 lashes, and it was
suggested that the sentences should be inflicted in
public, an admission of 25 cents being charged.
The prisoners agreed to the arrangement on con-
dition they should receive the gate-money. There
are a few tons of suggestiveness in this item, and
poor, hard-up Treasurer MTlwraith ought to wel-
come it as a means of filling the State coffers.
By the way, what are the State coffers ? The
Treasury of one Australian province once con-
sisted of an old portmanteau. Bromley, the naval
officer, lost the thing, and the “ colony ” set an
example—which has been followed times without
number since, by other colonies —of going bung.
Land at Perth, W. A., the best Investment.
Plans -from Intercolonial Investment Co., Ltd.
309 Pitt-street, Sydney.
B. Mountcastle and Sons have just re-
ceived the first of their regular fortnightly ship-
ments of Messrs. Cooksey and Co.’s celebrated
London hats, in all the latest shapes.*
An improved postal-box is being intro-
duced into Sydney simultaneously with an
improved Postmaster-General.
A Glasgow (N. 8.) church has got down so
low that it now raises the wind by means of a
soap advertisement pasted on the gallery.
A railway clerk at Junee (N.S.W.) has
had the audacity to object to continuous Sunday
work, and has been suspended accordingly.
Where is that eight-hours movement?
Brisbane Observer came out the other
day with headings in the following sequence—
ROCKHAMPTON RECORDS.
Good Sign.
Death of a Solicitor.
The Observer need not have expressed its
jubilation as brusquely as all that.
N.S. Wales is threatened with a new
curse. Parkes’ latest big portrait is to be repro-
duced as an oleograph. When this is done the
coming Protectionist Government will order a
large supply, and stick one up in every State
school throughout the land as an awful warning
to the coming generation, and as the surest means
of keeping Parkes out of office for all futurity.
The other day a man aged 70 was brought
up before Chief Justice Darley, charged with a
serious assault. The only defence was insanity,
and when this plea broke down the Chief Justice
congratulated the prisoner, in a friendly way, on
his narrow escape from lifelong confinement in an
asylum. Then, with a beaming smile, he sent
him to break stones and live in a whitewashed
cell for ten years, and seemed to expect that the
old wreck would look glad over it.
A Queensland paper tells an unfinished
story about a bush man who went round Brisbane
for several days drinking and swearing in company
with an affable stranger, whom he afterwards dis-
covered to be a lord. The story —like all such
stories —ends abruptly just when the lord revealed
himself. We presume that it is intended to con-
vey by this that the bushman fell paralysed into a
plate-glass window when he discovered that he
had been drinking with a peer—at all events the
climax seems to be taken for granted somehow.
The Triad (secret Chinese) society in the
Northern Territory of S.A. has at least 400
members, who pay annually a fee of from 10s. to
£2, according to their social status. It is the
refuge of criminals of all types. The Triad
defends its own members whenever accused in the
courts, and has spent as much as £4OO in defending
a criminal, while it has often, by manufactured
evidence, sent innocent people to gaol. It has
three known places of meeting—Palmerston, The
Union and Yam Creek. Its meetings are con-
vened by the sign of a certain flag, and it has
secret signals and pass-words.
The courteous American editor once more
goes for the man who runs the rival paper :
He is a squint-eyed, consumptive liar with the breath
of a buzzard and the record of a convict—a poor, sallow,
slimy lizard—a mildewed, corpse-faced creature—a red-
nosed scavenger with blue mould and the dropsy. We
have looked at him long and earnestly and recognised in
him the features of a razor-backed, lop-eared, sag-bellied,
corner-toed wolf-whelp. He is a fiendish atrocity, a
twisted, warped, livid, glass-eyed, greasy freak who com-
bines a two-weeks-old corpse in the river with a cross-
section of a morgue on a busy day.
This sort of thing would almost seem to imply
that he doesn’t like the other man.
Directly any sect of devout believers
emerges from thd chrysalis state of contempt or
persecution, and basks in the sun of popularity, it
begins to be implacable, remorseless, and per-
secuting. The latest exemplification of humility
grown respectable and aggressive was given at
North Shore (Sydney) not long ago, when a
“ capting ” gave one Gallagher, a mere boy, into
custody for demonstrative conduct within the
barracks, and, not content with incarcerating the
offender for 30 hours, pressed for his committal to
Quarter Sessions. Somebody suggested that the
captain’s conduct was unchristian-like ; but that’s
just where he was wrong. It was desperately and
horribly Christian.
The Melbourne Standard reports that
gold is so plentiful at Murchison (W.A.) that the
sheep which graze around there have chunks of it
sticking to their teeth. This looks promising,
but on the Thames (M.L.), in the old days of the
Caledonia mine, things were even better. Sheep
were scarce there, but horses were abundant, and
almost every one of them had a bank deposit
receipt for a large amount in his mouth. The
gold used to accumulate on the animal’s teeth to
such an extent that he had to take it to the bank
to get rid of it before he could eat his breakfast.
One horse got lost there for six months, and when
he was found again he had accumulated a canital
of £4500 which he had fixed for 12 months at 5 per
cent.
A correspondent writes to point out that
Australia badly wants a national song “ similar to
‘Yankee Doodle,’ with a good lively air to it.”
He kindly sends along the song. We quote one
verse as a sample of what the weary newspaper
man has to put up with :
The bold kangaroo he is pumpkins a few
As he springs off his tail, as he springs off his tail;
And the nations he will chase
When he put on the pace
And springs off his tail, and springs off his tail.
A London paper, re Mrs. Besant and her
awful falling-off among the Mahatmas and the
philosophers of the Hidden Way :
Mrs. Besant’s unfortunate relapse should be a warning
to those who would advocate that women, even of the
higher intellectual grade, are fit and proper persons to be
eiitmsted with the management of State affairs. Sup-
pose, for example, that Mrs. Besant had been entrusted
with the post of Home Secretary, and were to advise the
Queen as to the remission or otherwise of sentences
according to instructions received—by the penny post—
from the Thcosophical Mrs. ’Arris’s who are called
Mahatmas.
Also suppose, for example, that Colonel Olcott
had been made Home Secretary, or the deceased
and lamented Joe Smith with his revelations and
Ids “golden book,” or suppose a Mahatma got the
billet, or Milner Stephen with his red flannel and
his healing breath, or say that the wildly erratic
George Francis Train had it —what then ? Do not
all these possibilities show just as clearly that a
man shouldn’t have the job either ? And then, if
a woman isn’t fit to have a hand in State affairs,
and if a man isn’t fit either for precisely the same
rea'On, who is to be Home Secretary? Barring
the Missing Link, there is nothing left.

A Charnel-House Stench.
“ So you will have nothing to say to me, Aline
“ Nothing.”
“ Think—it is best to be my friend.”
“It is useless, Monsieur Vimard —please leave
me.”
“ Ah, I know your reason. Rene Brujere has
been speaking to you.”
“ Well, and if he has?”
“ Only this —I hate Rene! Come, I will give
you one more chance. Will you hear me?”
But Aline _ had left the cafe before he had
finished. Vimard smiled gently, and ordered
another absinthe.
Vimard and Rene Brujere were both students
at the celebrated medical schools of Paris. The
first was a clever man—absorbed all day in his
studies and lectures, and reputed to have an un-
canny taste for toxology. He was a regular
frequenter of the Cafe Maria, where he used to
sit all the evening drinking absinthe and smiling
sweetly.
. He and Rene Brujere had for some time been
rivals for Aline; she had always been most im-
partial with her smiles, but latterly seemed to
favour Rene. A happy, sunny youth was he, well
liked by his fellows and professors—a man of
brilliant parts—though at present he frequented
one of the fastest sets of the University. A man
of some determination, too—with a weakness for a
pretty face—which is not a paradox.
Vimard said nothing—he was amiable and
affable as ever, and his smile seemed to have
grown sweeter.
In a few weeks the lectures closed and the
steady plodders set to work with a will to study
for the final examination to be held in a short
month’s time. The methodical ones mapped out
their work beforehand—so many hundred pages
of notes per day—-and these were generally the
most successful. Others, fired with a somewhat
tardy zeal, locked themselves in their rooms, and
threw boots and keys out of the window—drew
up provisions with a basket and line—worked
with tremendous energy, and, after a week of black
coffee and wet towels, gave it up in despair, and
returned to the old life of piquet, absinthe, and
“ les demoiselles.”
Rene had disappeared from his old haunts and
could not be found—some said he was at Meudon
—others at Corbeille—his rooms were to let, and
his trunks gone. Strangely enough Aline had
also disappeared—which augured badly for Rene’s
diploma.
Vimard did not change his life one jot—heard
the discussions and rumours at the cafe —listened
with his usual smile and only changed his drink
from absinthe to sugar and water with orange
flowers.
The month passed all too quickly for the
workers, all too slow for the idlers.
The examinations usually lasted ten days or a
fortnight—ten days of suspense, of agony, of
anxious hopes and fears during which the little
world of the Quartier Latin was more agitated
than by a barricade or a revolution in the city.
Then one by one the victims stood up before the
awful bench of professors. One poor fellow
against a host of tormentors, torturing him and
probing him to find his weak points. No wonder
that he should flounder sometimes, and one can
sympathise with the man who, asked to give an
example of expansion of bodies by heat, answered,
“In-summer the days are longer than in winter !”
Rene, who had re-appeared, passed this pre-
liminary ordeal well; indeed, Vimard only sur-
passed him—the finals were at the end of the
week.
The next day the University seemed filled with
a vague stench—undefinable, but present and
loathsome.
Towards the evening it grew stronger—those in
the same hall as Rene seemed to feel it most—
professors, public, and students were annoyed and
disgusted—it had been a warm day they said, and
drains were not perfection even in the Home of
Science. Rene went home with the stench in his
nostrils—it seemed to pervade the whole street—
the very meat and drink were nauseous. When
he awoke next day, at dawn, he felt as if he were
suffocating—his room was reeking with the vile,
detestable smell, and when he threw open his
window it seemed worse. He rushed into the
street—horror! People fled from him on every
side with loathing. God ! It was a horrible,
fearful, noisome stench ! the very horses and
dogs shrank from him, and where he passed they
sprinkled ammonia and disinfectants.
He thought the people must be mad. He felt
ms brain reeling with the over-powering vapours.
He shouted, and ran to speak to some men. They
paused ten paces away—then turned to flee with
their handkerchiefs to their noses. At last the
poor wretch realised that this pestilence, this
hell-stink, came from him—from him alone—it
was he who stank ! It was from his body that the
horrible stench came ! And he sat on the kerb
and groaned and loathed himself.
Pretty soon he heard a window open and felt
the impact of a hard object, whilst a voice cried :
Away, thou pestilence—away, thou leper, thou
walking corpse—go bury thyself again !”
Half-mad, with a nameless dread in his breast
and the stench of ton thousand carrions in his
mouth, he bent his head and fled away, through
streets and boulevards to the green fields and
trees. No one thought of stopping him—the sen-
tinels drew back horrified, and one raised his
me to shoot.
P antin ff> fetching with nausea, he
stumbled and fell in a coppice.
He must have lain some time fainting, for he
was aroused by a sharp nip, and found a hungry
Pig trying his calf—a pig—the only living thing
inat could approach him. Por three days and
iee nights he wandered in the woods, hiding at
day-time lest the keepers should find him, and
mg like some savage beast on roots and berries,
n the fourth day, one of the keepers, on going
a.i S caught the taint on the wind—with
aic ! dog he soon found the cause. Rene

Well, the “scent” left him in the same unac-
countable way it came—he obtained leave to pass
again, under his exceptional circumstances, and
passed well—his strange malady was the talk of
the town for a few days till a new sensation came
up.
Yimard? Well, he passed too, and gained a
ward in the Hospital for his able and remarkable
paper “on the effects of tellurium salts on the
human body.”
Aline? Oh —of course she found a consoler
after the first two days of Rene’s absence. No,
not Yimard.
The local doctor in a little town on one of
the rivers of N.S.W. has a wife, and the wife has
about eleven dogs. She is a lady of massive con-
struction, with a carnivorous expression and a
devouring eye, and when her husband starts on
his rounds she accompanies him. So do the dogs.
When the old man—a harmless individual with
an obliterated aspect—goes in to diagnose an
enlarged liver, or to fix a broken leg, she goes
along with him, and the dogs wait outside. She
also hustles round and looks at tumours and
scalds, and offers suggestions, and she discusses
the patient’s condition in an affable, unembarrassed
way that makes that sufferer long to attend her
funeral. Probably she means well, but a nervous
patient doesn’t want to be visited by one doctor,
a woman, and eleven dogs all in one act. It is
too overwhelming.
The solitary constable stationed at Ayton,
near Cooktown, N.Q., recently arrested an alleged
murderer and had him up before the local beaks.
The functionary stepped forward to sign his
depositions ; the prisoner rushed for the open
door. The functionary collared him on the
threshold, and the prisoner rounded on him, and
heaved him across the room. His nose made a
dent in the wall and gore incarnadined the
precincts, but he nobly re-collared the prisoner,
and both rolled over in the shambles, and
wallowed amid the ruins. The officer called upon
the Bench in the Queen’s name, and Dogberry—
two of him—fell upon the struggling heap of
policeman and prisoner, and secured the latter at
the cost of being tattooed all over with kicks and
disfigurements. One Dogberry wrote to the
Colonial Secretary narrating the above incidents
and asking for a second policeman at Ayton. The
Colonial Secretary pithily remarked that “there
appears to be a certain amount of justice ” in
the request. However, the Government has not
yet heard the prisoner’s opinion on the subject.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*

price as tea might, or beef. In England gold—which
keeps very steady in price—is legal tender—in other
words, anybody can always get gold for his silver, and that
keeps the latter firm. But for that the English shilling
would vary between Bd. and 9d. according to the value of

When three Judges have to sit together
and decide upon a “ point ” it is almost invariably
found that two Judges think one way, and the
third Judge thinks the opposite. Eryo the Law
is an untrustworthy ass, unable to interpret
itself.
A Frenchman, with a noble eye for artistic
effect, one night recently climbed half-way up the
Eiffel Tower, fixed the end of about 100 yards of
rope round his neck, made the other end fast to
the tower, and then fled through the air and the
darkness till the noose brought him up. He was
found there in the morning, and it was noticed,
when his remains were brought down, that he had
hung himself in correct evening costume. Even
in his last moments he had an eye to the eternal
fitness of things.
The S.M. Herald girds with especial
rancour at Edmund Barton for belonging to a
Ministry which proposes to postpone the “ great
question of Federation ” till next year. The
Herald didn’t mind Parkes postponing it, so long
as events didn’t postpone Parkes. But now the
Herald wants Federation for early breakfast
without fail. The Herald knows heavy legisla-
tion is a physical impossibility this year. The
election-writs are not finally returnable till 28th
November, though the House has been only
adjourned until the 18th. The Herald’s own
calculation is that there can only be nineteen
sittings before Christmas Eve. Yet, with the
estimates to be passed, and with several measures
of absolute necessity to be considered, the
Herald wants Federation jammed through
regardless of impossibilities. The fact is, the
question must be postponed. Under the circum-
stances, even a measure for the burning of the
Herald by a specially uncommon hangman, and
the execution of the editor, though it might be a
Bill of pressing public need, would have to be put
off for a,season.

Committee on Railways was the other day ques-
tioned by a friend anent a recent visit to Gipps-
land : “Is there any coal there, or is it only
mud ?” “ Both,” was the laconic reply.
Rev. Hugh Gihnour, an Adelaide demo-
crat parson of the Primitive Methodist persuasion,
died on 23rd October, aged 42 years. The orthodox
parson whose life is one generous fattening process
of leisured laziness usually lives into his seventies
and eighties ; the man who, like Gilmour, worries
over the hardships and grievances of the poor,
dies old and worn Out in what should have been
his prime.
W. E. Abbott, the capitalist whose tale of
woe has slopped over daily for the last six months
in the correspondence columns of the Australian
daily press, now writes to the London Morning
Post to state that Dibbs and Parker are both
truckling to the Labour-party —“ a coterie of
fools and knaves”—for. the sake of office. Mr.
Abbott is hereby informed that he is a failure and
a grievous waste of ink. He is only a fly on the
end of a blade of grass shaking its fist at the stars,
and he isn't a large fly, either—only an ordinary,
middle-sized one.
General Giovamunnelli, a Mexican fire-
eater now in the French service, is the hero of a
gory narrative. In the thick of an engagement
and under a heavy fire he was explaining to his
comrades the details of some difficult military
mameuvre when —
Suddenly the officer on his right whom he was especi-
ally addressing fell dead with a bullet through his brain.
Without apparently noticing the incident, the General
turned to his left-hand neighbour and continued his ex-
planation. A minute later this second officer also
dropped, and Giovanninnelli, still imperturbable, was
turning to a third officer to complete his tactical lecture,
when this one also fell dead with a ball in his chest,
“ Whether the General ever completed his remarks,”
observes the officer who relates the anecdote, “ I cannot
say. All I know is that I did not, remain to listen any
longer.”
“ At a crisis like this,” said the Man with
the Piercing Orbs, “at the parting of thejways,”
as the. philosopher puts it, at the climax of con-
vulsion when even Parkes’ Hair cannot escape
the deadly clutch of the influenza, and when
Dibbs and Edmund Barton toss up for first cavort
in the coming Carmagnole, let the purblind mole
of the momentary now swear by his unchristened
(because unborn) fetiches and yell cacophonously
for free-trade or fritteration, as the case
may be ; let the mugmump lie stertorously
in his golgotha, pawing over the desic-
cated relics of his vanished victims; let the
geebung scintillate vainly out of his own embers ;
let earth howl for soup, and ocean mourn for dry-
hash ; but let me, as a calm, rational, and level-
beaded cornstalk, whoop with all my lungs for
that motherly oil of commerce known as John
Robertson’s whisky, or, more lovingly and
familiarly, as ‘ J.D.R.’—Johnny, the flower of
Dundee.

The Land of the Crocodile.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I hero is probably nothing which more
effectually induces tlie true citizen of this great
country to feel round for a tomahawk or an iron-
bark nulla than reading an account of a meeting
of “ representative Australians ”in London. The
infatuated mortal who made a fortune by acci-
dent in a rich reef, or held a tract of squatting
country as big as a European kinerdom under a
£lO license away back in the forties or fifties, still
cherishes a hazy delusion that lie represents Aus-
tralia and Australians in some way or other not
clearly defined, though his habits and thoughts
are about as much in accord with the Australian’s
as those of a clay-eating Indian or a blubber-
devouring Esquimo.
* * #
Prominent among these misguided fossil
relics of our early barbarous days, are some of the
“ °W squatters ” who wont “ home ” 30 or 40 years
ago to grow fat and useless on Australian money
while they gossip about the good old days and
continue to advise the Imperial Government how
Australia of the present should bo governed.
* * *
And this reminds me that when compiling
my coming history of the Darling Downs and the
6ciily sc]ujittors, there c.\me into my possession a
copy of a letter sent by Christopher Rolleslon,
the first Crown Lands Commissioner on the
Downs, to Arthur Hodgson, the present Sir
Arthur, one of our “representative colonists”
A his letter is dated on April !), 1845, from Cam-
booya, the then Border police-station.
therein informs Hodgson that lie has accepted 'his
tender for supplying rations to the Border police
at Is. per day, and forage at Gd. per ration of 41b.
of corn daily. Hodgson’s sureties, guaranteeing
that he would carryout his contract, were Dom-
viHe, Taylor, and David Forbes.
* * *
Hodgson was always in trouble with his
men, w.,0 were nearly all assigned convicts. He
was one of the most regular prosecutors at the
C.uubooya Police-court. The working free then
ami ex-convicts had a hard time in those days.
The, first had their wages forfeited and the others
got the lash. On the 17th Dec., 1844, a ticket-of-
leave man named Jas. Cannon, ex ship John, in
18311, was brought up at the Cambooya Police
Court, before Christopher Rolleston, David Forbes
and Arthur Hodgson, charged by Mark Halo, the
chief constable and overseer, with insolence and
disobedience of orders. There was no other
evidence. The offence would be punished to-day
by a fine of ss. The following is the verdict
“The Court sentence the prisoner to a hundred
lashes—7s on the back and 25 on the breech.” I
attach an exact copy of all three magistrates’
signatures, and that of Hale, signed on the verdict
in the original deposition-book, which is in my
possession, along with still more interesting manu-
scripts of the early days.
# * «
One of Burke and Wills’ camels came in
af ter ward s f rom Cooper’s Creek to Wyenbah stati on.
A mile from the homestead a blackfellow was
herding a mob of cattle. He was lying down
half asleep holding the bridle of his horse. A wild
rush among the cattle and a frenzied snort from
his steed suddenly brought the myall to an erect
position. The first sight he beheld was the camel
standmg; about five yards away gazing at him
with a mournful but amiable expression, which, in
plain language meant “dear mother, I’ve come
home to die.” The myall only gave one glance at
that awfijl and previously unheard-of apparition,
seen only in dreams begotten of a late supper of
roast bear, and then he struck out for the home-
stead, first flinging away his bridle under the im-
pression that his horse wouldn’t travel fast enough
for the occasion. Every 50 yards he looked round,
only to see the camel trotting peacefully along in
the rear, a close second, and then on each occasion
there was a louder yell and an increase in
the pace. When the myall reached the house he
dived through the window, carrying away the
glass and half the sash, and crawled under the
dining-room table, whence he took a scared peep
to see if the devil hadn’t followed him through
the keyhole. And then Mrs. Eazey, who chanced
to be at home, enquired what was the matter, and
eventually Billy came slowly and cautiously from
under the table to explain that out at the front
door there was something half horse and half
emu, but that nothing like it had ever been seen
before in Billy’s country. Billy never recovered
from the shock.
• * *
The Rev. J. G. Gribble, the originator of
the Warafigesda Mission in N.S.W., is at present
in Queensland on the look-out for a suitable site
for a mission-station among the blacks in the
Cairns district. Gribble is a thin, dark, sombre-
looking man whose gaunt framework offers no
temptation to even the most rabid cannibal,
except those specially addicted to eating bones.
He tells a good story of an incident on a journey
from Albnry to Sydney. The mail-train was
passing across some unoccupied country where
station accommodation was represented by a soli-
tary platform, where any lonely bushman anxious
to extricate himself from the surrounding soli-
tude could stop an ordinary train by wildly
waving a red tin-banner and executing a
war-dance. As the mail-train approached
this exciting locality a woman was observed
brandishing the tin ensign and making frantic
gesticulations to the driver to stop. The driver
and guard being under the impression that several
budges were down, or a mile of line was uprooted
by an earthquake, or a mountain had fallen over
the track, brought the tram to a standstill about
a hundred yards past the platform. Then the
excited lady, who had a red head and wore blucher
boots, rushed by the astonished guard and going
a grey-headed old fossil who was yelling
Hole, Martha ! from th© window, hand©d him
a live rooster and a bunch of flowers and fled ! The
rage of the guard and the driver, and the language
they employed to express their emotions, must be
left to those who have fraternised with the pioneer
bullock-drivers of the old days.

“Victor Second.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We were training two horses for the
Buckatowndown races. An old grey warrior
called Tricolor, whom the station boys insisted on
calling “The Trickier,” and a mare for .the hack
race, fetation horses don’t get trained quite like
Carbine : some days we had no time to give them
their gallops at all, so they had to gallop twice as
far the next day to make up. And one day the
boy we had looking after The Trickier fell in witli
a mob of sharps who told him we didn’t know
anything about training horses, and that what the
horse really wanted was “a twicer,” that is to
say, a gallop twice round the course, feo the boy
gave him “a twicer” on his own responsibility,
and when we found out about it wo gave the boy a
twicer with the strap, and he left and took out a
summons against us for assault. But somehow or
another we managed to get the old horse pretty
fit, and trying him against hacks of different
descriptions we persuaded ourselves that we had
the biggest certainty ever known on a race-course.
* * *
When the horses were galloping in the
morning the kangaroo-dog Victor used nearly
always go down to the course and run round with
them. It amused him apparently and didn’t hurt
anyone, so we used to let him race; in fact, we
rather encouraged him because it kept him in good
trim to hunt kangaroos. When we were starting
the horses away for the meeting someone said- we
had better tie up the dog or he would be getting
stolen at the races. We called and whistled but
he had made himself scarce, and we started off
and forgot all about him.
* * #
Buckatowndown Races. Red - hot day,
everything dusty, everybody drunk and blasphem-
ous. All the betting at Buckatowndown was
double-event. You had to win the money first,
and fight the man for it afterwards. The start
for our race, the Town Plate, was delayed for a
quarter of an hour, because the starter flatly
refused to leave a fight of which lie was an
interested spectator. Every horse, as he did his
preliminary gallop, had a string of dogs after him,
and the clerk of the course came full cry after the
dogs with a whip. By and by the horses strung
across to the start at the far side of the course.
They fiddled about for a bit, and then down went
the flag and they came sweeping along all bunched
up together, one moke holding a nice position on
the inside. All of a sudden we heard a wild
chorus of imprecations—“ Look at that dog!”
Our dog had made his appearance and had chipped
in with the racehorses and was running right in
front of the field. It looked a guinea to a
gooseberry that some of them would fall on him.
The owners danced and swore in awful style!
What did we mean by bringing a something
mongrel there to trip up and kill horses that were
worth a paddockfull of all the horses we had ever
owned, or ever would breed or own, even if wo
lived to be a thousand ? We were fairly in it and
no mistake. As the field came past the stand the
first time we could hear the riders swearing at the
dog, and a wild yell of execration arose from the
public. Ho had got right among the ruck by this
time, and was racing alongside his friend The
Trickier, thoroughly enjoying himself. After
passing the stand the pace became very merry
and the dog stretched out all ho knew, and when
they began to make it too hot for him he cut off
corners, and joined at odd intervals, and every
time he made a fresh appearance the people in the
stand lifted up their voices and “ swore cruel ”as
the boys phrased it. The horses were all at the
whip as they turned into the straight, and then
old tricolor and the publican’s mare singled out.
Wo could hear the “ chop, c]iop ! ” of the whips as
they came along together, but the mare could
suffer it as long as the old fellow could, and she
swerved off and he struggled home a winner by a
length or so. Just as they settled down to finish
the dog dashed up on the inside, and passed the
post at old Tricolor’s girths. The populace im-
mediately took to him with stones, bottles, and
other missiles, and he had to scratch gravel to
save his life. What was the amazement then of
the other owners to learn that the judge had
placed Tricolor first, Victor second, and the
publican’s mare third ?
* # #
The publican tried to argue it out with
him. He said you couldn’t place a kangaroo-dog
second in a horserace. The judge said that it
was his (hiccough) business what he placed, and
that those who (hiccough) interfered with him
would be sorry for it. Also he expressed the
opinion, garnished with a fusilade of curses and
hiccoughs, that the publican’s mare was no rotten
good, and that she was the right sort of mare for
a poor man to own, because she would keep him
poor. 1 hen the publican called the judge a cow,
and the judge being willing, a rip, tear, and chew
fight ensued, which lasted some time and the
judge won. There were 15 protests lodged against
our win, but we didn’t have any fear of these
going against us—we had laid the stewards a bit
.We got away with our horses at once
didn t w r ait for the hack race. Every second
man we met wanted to run us a mile for £lOO
aside, and there was a drunken shearer who was
spoiling for a fight, and he said he had heard we
were brimming over with science.” and he
had ridden forty miles to find whether it was a
fact or not. We folded our tents Tike the Arab
and stole away and left the point unsettled. But
i remains on the annals of Buckatowndown how a
kangaroo-dog ran second for the Town Plate.

The Digger’s Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scrape the bottom of the hole, gather up the stuff,
Fossick in the crannies, lest you leave a grain
behind,
Just another shovelful and that’ll be enough,
Now we’ll take it to the bank and see what wo
can find,
Give the dish a twirl around,
Let the water swirl around.
Gently let it circulate, there’s music in the swish,
And the twinkle of the gravel,
As the pebbles quickly travel
Around in merry circles on the bottom of the dish.
Ah, if man could wash his life, if he only could,
Panning off the evil deeds, keeping but the
good.
What a mighty lot of digger’s dishes would be
sold,
Tho’ I fear the lot of tailings would be greater
than the gold,
Give the dish a twirl around,
Let the water swirl around,
Man’s the sport of circumstance however he may
wish,
Fortune, are you there now ?
Answer to ray prayer now,
And drop a. half-ounce nugget in the bottom of
the dish.
Gently let the water lap, keep the corners dry,
That’s about the place thegold’ll generally stay,
What vvas that bright particle that just then
caught my eye ?
I fear me by the look of things ’twas only
yellow clay,
Just another twirl around,
Let the water swiil around,
That’s the way wo rob the river of its golden fish,
What’s that ? can’t we snare a one?
Don’t say that there’s ne’er a one,
Bah, there’s not a colour in the bottom of the
dish.
"SURCINGLE."
George Augustus Sala the other day
privately related that while waiting in the office
of a Yankee “Palace” hotel, where he intended
putting up, and watching the clerk entering his
pedigree, brands,- &c., he noticed some dark
objects moving across the ledger. Becoming sus-
picious, he asked, “What are those?” “Bugs,”
laconically replied the ledgerman. “ What are
they doing there?” “Guess they’re waiting to
see the number of your room !”
Mr. Newman, N.S. W. Frectrade politician,
has made of himself a finished and complete
public ass, and his ears stand up in bold relief
against the sky. He wanted a park for his con-
stituents at East Orange, and therefore he seems
to have requested his friends there to write a
letter abusing him, and threatening to throw him
out if he didn’t get it. Then he went to the
Minister of Lands and showed him the document,
and tearfully told him that when his constituents
had started to heave language like this, things
were desperate, and he must either get the park
or else join Dibbs and become a Protectionist.
Then he wrote to his constituents with a whoop of
joy, telling them that the thing had come off.
Lastly, some base miscreant got hold of the whole
story and published it. Finally, as already men-
tioned, Newman became an ass.
Speaking of the pious frenzy which has
arisen in Victoria the S.M. Herald wisely
observes: —
The fact that our friends in Victoria have been some"
times regarded as devotees to the varied mysteries of the
worship of Mammon is nothing at all against the pos-
sibility of their going in earnestly on this new line; for,
paradoxical though it may look, these two divergent and
dissimilar tendencies are not infrequently found together
in human nature.
True, O bulky and moral paper ! Salvation and
the accumulation of cash do go together, and
when the average sinner is “saved ” his cat gets
no fatter and his dog doesn’t get a square feed
any oftener, and his employes don’t got their
wages raised, and his miserable horse is galloped
off its four legs just as before. Therefore when
we meet an alleged Christian we always go round
to his back premises and ask his horse if he is
genuine, and if that animal reports unfavourably
upon him we accept its verdict as conclusive.
Two thousand six hundred per shent was
all a bashful Brisbane man, one Cohen, could
find it in his heart to ask from another pork-
avoider, for a nineteen-day loan of £250. When
time was called, the lender put the bailiffs on the
borrower, and the borrower put a head on the
lender in return. Then Papa Pinnock, P.M.,
listened to their tale of woe, and the Israelites
went up into the temple to pray, for it was the
festival of the “Ten Days of Atonement,” that
ushers in the Jewish year 5652. How they do
love each other!
When Israel of the Lord beloved
Prom onj the land of bandage went,
Emblazoned on their banners flew
This motto, “ shent per shent.”
The vanguard bore three brazen bails,
Aloft on poles, to represent
The Hebrew fiscal policy
And motto “ shent per shent.”
By night they snored the old refrain,
And flapped the fly and shook the lent;
By day they sang their song of praise
And chorussed “shent per shent.”
For forty years behind.their “ cofiks ”
They tramped and mused on shekels lent
To “shentellmen ” in Gentile lands
And copped their “ shent per shent.”
The cloudy guide was all my eye,
The fiery column only meant
To indicate a Promised Land
Of balmy “ shent per shent.”
A Promised Land of noses straight •
To feed the push with noses bent,
And cry, “ What price a little loan V”
“Veil, only ‘ shent per shent ’!”
A Promised Land of addled heads
To haunt the signs of “ money lent, '
Who pawn their souls to heaven or hell
At any price “per shent.”

A Sydney Salvationist convert lately said :
—“ I would have killed a man in a bar brawl,
but when I struck him on the head with a crow-
bar the Lord restrained the force of my arm, so I
only stunned him.” Now, the Deity did not
interfere, for most likely the Deity superintended
the formation of the man’s skull, and when it was
finished the Deity rested, remarking that it was a
lovely nut, dense as an anvil, and that nothing
less than a steam-hammer would crack it.
“There are three codes of morals — one
for women, one for men, and one for Royalty.
Women must be virtuous or be socially damned ;
men may do as they please, provided they pay
their club-bills and do not cheat at cards; Royalty
—well, there’s a divinity doth hedge a royal
person which makes of his orgies very proper tea-
parties. The one sympathetic link in these
several codes is that nobody—not even a woman —
loses caste unless found out.” —Amek.
THEY ROLLED ON THE FLOOR.
On Monday, November 24tb, 1890, the American
papers published the following news item : —
“Mrs. Sarah S. Hensler, of No. 873, East One
Hundred-and-Thirty-fourth-street, New York,
shot and* killed herself yesterday morning. She
was a lady of excellent character and high social
position, and a member of the Rev. Dr. Ramsay’s
Presbyterian Church. She was well-to-do and
very active in various public and private charities.
Since last July she had suffered fearfully from
indigestion and dyspepsia, which brought on
melancholia, and then a kind of insanity, under
the influence of which she took her own life.”
Here is another story, not so tragical, but with
the same moral. The narrator tells it of himself.
“Mostly,” he says, “we dread and fear death,
yet once I prayed to die, and the reason was in
this wise. Up to Christmas, 1888, I had been a
healthy man, but at that time (a period of rejoicing
with so many) I felt depressed, languid, and tired.
My appetite left me, and I was much distressed
after eating the lightest food. My skin and eyes
became tinged with a dark yellow colour, and the
kidney secretion was like blood. The pain in my
stomach was almost unbearable and often lasted
from 12 to 14 hours without intermission. Some-
times I was in pain night and day, and was so bad
that my wife had to sit up with me through the
night. I was constantly sick, and troubled with
a stomach cough, and expectorated a quantity of
green phlegm. f
“ In spite of warm clothing and every comfort,
I was always chilled ; the cold shivers running
through me as if my blood were thin and poor. I
could take uo solid food ; I lived on soups, milk,
puddings, &c., and after each meal I had empty,
gnawing, windy pains at the stomach, wliich
nothing relieved.
“ After a time an intolerable itching of the skin
spread all over me, as if my blood were poisoned.
Our family physician attended mo for about a
year. Acting upon his advice I went to Harro-
gate, where I consulted another doctor, and drank
the Waters, but feeling worse 1 returned home.
The bath attendant at Harrogate and others told
me I was suffering from blood poisoning, but this
the doctors never mentioned. The first doctor
said it was the passing of gallstones that gave me
such dreadful pain.
“ I now consulted an eminent specialist at Man-
chester, who confirmed what the other doctor had
told mo, but none of them afforded me any relief.
“In this miserable way I dragged on for six
months more, and became so much reduced I
could scarcqjy put one foot before the other, and
so thin thatJ/iC rings fell off my fingers and rolled
on the floor. I was in such pain that I prayed to
die, and one of the doctors told a friend of mine I
could not recover.
“ In August qf last year (1890), whilst my suffer-
ings were at the worst, a book was sent to me by
post, telling of a medicine called Mother Sejgel's
Curative Syrup. I determined to try it, and sent
to Mr. Evans, Chemist, Lymm, for a supply.
After taking the first bottle, I felt a little better,
and by persevering with the remedy I recovered
my appetite, and gradually gained strength. My
natural colour is now returned, and I feel as well
as I ever did in my life ; in fact, as well as I did
when a boy. I can eat any kind of food without
inconvenience, and Jrtive gained 30 pounds in
weight during the past three months. I may add
that previous to taking this medicine 1 was so
much altered that my friends, and even my pupils,
scarcely recognised me. I tell every one what
Seigel’s Syrup did for me. ”
The gentleman who makes the foregoing state-
ment is a person of position and known to all the
people of Lymm. He declines to permit the publi-
cation of his name, but the perfect truth of what
is here related is vouched for by Mr. J.'H. Evans,
the chemist above named.
The case was an aggravated one of indigestion
and dyspepsia and its natural consequence. The
whole system had been poisoned and disordered by
the acids engendered by the fermentation in the
stomach, and, had not Seigel’s Syrup come to the.
rescue just as it did a fatal result must have
followed in a brief time.

The Literary Friend.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Once I wrote a little poem which I thought was
very fine,
And I showed the printer’s copy to a critic friend
of mine,
1 irst he praised the thing a little, then he found a
little fault;
“The ideas are good,” he muttered, “but the
rhythm seems to halt.”
So I straighten’d up the rhythm where he marked
it with his pen,
And I copied it and showed it to my clever friend
again.
“You’ve improved the metre greatly, but the
rhymes are bad,” he, said,
As he read it slowly, scratching surplus wisdom
from his head.
So I worked as he suggested (I believe in taking
time),
And I burnt the “midnight taper” while I
straightened up the rhyme.
“It is better now,” he muttered, “you go on and
you’ll succeed,
It has got a ring about it—the ideas are what you
need. ”
So I worked for hours upon it (I go on when I
commence),
And I kept in view the rhythm and the jingle and
the sense,
And I copied it and took it to my solemn friend
once more,
But it ’minded him of something he had some-
where read before.
Now the people say I’d never pub such horrors into
print
If I wasn’t too conceited to accept a friendly hint,
And my dearest friends are certain that I’d profit
in the end
If I’d always show my copy to a literary friend.
Sydney, Oct., ’9l.

Gertrude Atherton says that people in-
capable of caring for themselves and crippled
children should have their heads stuck in a barrel
of chloroform, out of pure Christian charity.
Melbourne Evening Standard, during
two and a-half years, was served with 50 writs of
libel, of which only two ever came into court,
and those two were breakdowns, without the
defence being troubled to reply. The ex.’s of the
threatening* totalled over £400, of which not a
penny is recoverable, because the pretended prose-
cutors were all men of straw, not jmssessed of the
spare price of a shave.
One Knitt, a temperance lecturer, has
been lulling forth at Napier (M.L.) on the
subject of drink, and how it undermines the con-
stitution, and ruins the nerves, and makes the
nose of the drinker huge and red and horrible.
Therefore, Gorman, the leading publican, an-
nounces in the local paper that he will run Knitt
100 yards for a reasonable wager, in order to show
that drink doesn’t undermine the constitution so
much as teetotalism ; or he will swim Knitt half-
a-inile, or row him five miles, or drive a four-in
hand against him, or, failing all these things, he
will ride a steeplechase with Knitt. Further, he
is willing to bet £50 that lie is a better teetotaller
than Knitt; and, in addition to all these things,
he offers to submit Jus-nose to the inspection
12 respectable citizens if Knitt will Produce his
nose also, and he guarantees that Kttitt’s nhse is
the redder and the more swollen' and disgusting
proboscis of the two, and the sort of thing that no
pure and innocent girl ought to look at. Last of
all, and apart from the nose, ho bets that Knitt
looks more like a bloated drunkard than he does—
that he is shakier, flabbier, more pimpled, and
more unwholesome generally; and he asks that
this question should also be submitted to the jury.
Up to date Knitt hasn’t answered. *
Dear Bulletin,— “ Clothes make the man
the want of.them the fellow.” How often do we
see this illustrated in our towns, when a dead-
broke but well-dressed man sails along beautifully,
while he in moles, who pays his way, is cold-
shouldered ! Anyone who doubt's that clothes
make the man, should travel the bakOlox Well-
dressed, and his doubts will soon disappear.

An American gentleman has just con-
fessed that he and six other miners murdered a
party of 24 Chinese in Oregon four years ago and
robbed them of 60,000 dol. in gold dust. Aus-
tralia s Champion Chinese-shooter has not yet
been legally found out, but he is in business all
the same, and many a Chow has lost the number
of his mess and his bag of stuff per medium of
our friend’s unerring Smith-and-Wesson. Just
you ask any old Northern miner the name of the
man who lives by shooting Chinamen.

religiously insane reminds us that Parkes was
once in business in Geelong, and there wrote :—
A passing stranger in thy streets,

Whom none will bless of all he meets,
His blessing pours on thee, Geelong.

Says Schopenhauer ; “ That woman is by
nature meant to obey is evinced in the fact that
every woman who is placed in the unnatural
position of complete independence immediately
attaches Iterself to some man, by whom she allows
herself to be ruled. If she he young it will be a
lover; if she be old, a priest.” 'A Melbourne
worntheotherday saw her husband hanging him-
self. What did she do? Rejoice in her independ-
ence? Get the carving-knife and cut him down ?
Iso fear. She shrieked for the inevitable man
who came aldng and did the cutting.

A vigorous correspondent in the North
sends us a, series of, biographical sketches about
the principal politicians, Judges, and other public
men of Queensland. Looking over them
casually we find that one of the leading
members of the Upper House is an escaped
murderer who entered the ' country on
a stolen horse and carrying a-set of stolen
branding-irons. Another prominent member was
ehucker-oiit in an opium-den in l8f)G, and now
owns a lot of property, all of which is let to
Chinamen; and a friend of his /was wanted
by the years ago for a brutal outrage
but succeeded in squaring the case. A local judge
is said to run a harem, and pay the expenses
chiefly by taking cash insults ffofii wealthy
offenders, and a well-known M.L. A is accused of
having made his first rise by shooting diggers in
the Far North and plundering their mangled
remains. . The remainder of the two Houses con-
sists, apparently, of incurable drunkards who
started life by coining had money and stealing
cows. He is a vigorous correspondent with a
bright and flowing style, but is much too expen-
sive to print in detail.

What folly it would bo to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
ago as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
lor all scouring and house-cleaning.

In the United States, as elsewhere, the
